Building Indus. Ass'n v. Manheim Twp.


No. 674 C.D. 1996, No. 666 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


710 A.2d 141; 1998 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 246


September 13, 1996, Argued  
April 14, 1998, Decided  
April 14, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 4668 of 1993. Common Pleas Court of the County of Lancaster. Judge PEREZOUS.



DISPOSITION: The Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County is affirmed in part and reversed and remanded in part. The cross-appeal of Manheim Township is hereby dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant association sought review of an order of the Common Pleas Court, Lancaster County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed its declaratory judgment action challenging an ordinance of appellee township, which imposed an impact fee on developers. The lower court concluded that appellant lacked standing to challenge the ordinance, and was further barred under the defense of laches.


OVERVIEW: Appellant association filed a declaratory judgment action against appellee township challenging an ordinance imposing impact fees on developers. Also, appellant sought a refund of all collected impact fees. The lower court dismissed appellant's action, finding that it lacked standing to appeal and was barred under the defense of laches. On review, the court held that appellant did have standing to challenge the ordinance requiring impact fees because it established that at least one of its members had or would suffer a direct, immediate, or substantial injury as a consequence of the ordinance where the constituent members of appellant were taxpayers. Furthermore, the court found that appellant's action was not barred by the defense of laches because appellee failed to demonstrate any prejudice as a result of appellant's delay. Finally, the court found that appellant was not entitled to a refund of all collected impact fees because the fees were individualized damages that had to be collected by appellant's individual members. The court affirmed the order denying appellant a refund, but reversed the order finding that appellant lacked standing and was barred under defense of laches.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying appellant association a refund, but reversed the dismissal of appellant's declaratory judgment action against appellee township challenging an ordinance imposing impact fees on developers, holding that appellant had standing to challenge the ordinance because at least one of its members, as taxpayers, had or would suffer a direct, immediate, or substantial injury as a consequence of the ordinance.

 

473 of 892 DOCUMENTS


In re Nomination of Berg


No. 250 M.D. 1998


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


712 A.2d 340; 1998 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 262


March 30, 1998, Heard  
April 7, 1998, Decided  
April 7, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Memorandum Opinion Redesignated Opinion and Ordered Published April 21, 1998.



DISPOSITION: Petition GRANTED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner challenger sought to set aside the nomination of respondent candidate for the office of governor and respondent moved the court (Pennsylvania) to dismiss the petition. The issues were whether 25 P.S. § 2872.1(3) was constitutional and whether respondent had complied with its requirements by obtaining 100 valid signatures from Democratic electors of 10 counties within the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner challenger sought to set aside the nomination of respondent candidate for the office of governor and respondent moved the court to dismiss the petition. Concerning respondent's constitutional argument that 25 P.S. § 2872.1(3) violated the "one man-one vote principle" by diluting the signatures of electors in the more populous areas, the court first determined what standard of review it should apply. The court held that the one man-one vote principle was not involved, and, therefore, neither was a fundamental right. Applying the rational basis standard, which was more applicable given the greater deference and leeway extended to state elections as compared with the scrutiny of federal election cases, the court held that the state had several legitimate interests in the signature requirements of § 2872.1(3). The court held that respondent had failed to demonstrate that § 2872.1(3) was not rationally related to the furtherance of the state's interests. Therefore, the statute was constitutional. The court denied respondent's motion to dismiss. Respondent had stipulated that he did not obtain the signatures required by the statute, so the court set aside his nomination.


OUTCOME: The court denied respondent candidate's motion to dismiss and set aside his nomination. The statute was constitutional. Applying the rational basis test, the state had legitimate interests in the signature requirements of the statute. Respondent did not demonstrate that the statute was not rationally related to the furtherance of those interests. Respondent agreed that he did not comply with the statute; thus, his was nomination set aside.

Schnupp v. Port Auth.


No. 2651 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


710 A.2d 1235; 1998 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 233


November 6, 1997, Argued  
April 2, 1998, Decided  
April 2, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. GD94-12379. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Judge JAMES.



DISPOSITION: Reversed the trial court's order granting Appellees' motion for summary judgment and remanded the matter for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant sought review of an order of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny (Pennsylvania) that granted appellee port authority and employees' motion for summary judgment and dismissed appellant's complaint with prejudice, in appellant's action against appellees, alleging intentional infliction of emotional distress, misuse and abuse of process, assault and battery, false arrest, false imprisonment, libel, and slander.


OVERVIEW: Appellant claimant was mistreated by appellee port authority and employees after he argued with a trolley operator. He instituted an action against appellees alleging intentional infliction of emotional distress and other offenses. Appellees filed a motion for summary judgment, which the trial court granted. Appellant sought review and the court reversed the order. Appellant argued that the trial court erred in granting appellees' motion because he alleged a course of conduct by appellees acting under color of law which, on its face, established that they had violated his civil rights. The court found that additional discovery would likely uncover material facts relevant to establishing whether the conduct would give rise to a cause of action against them under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. Therefore, the trial court erred in granting appellees' motion. Appellant claimed that the trial court erred in granting appellees' motion on the basis that sovereign immunity shielded them from liability. An employee was not protected by the local agency's immunity if his act constituted a crime. Appellees, therefore, were not with respect to the count of intentional infliction of emotional distress.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court that granted appellee port authority and employees' motion for summary judgment in appellant claimant's action against them for intentional infliction of emotional distress and other offenses, where the court found additional discovery was likely to provide material facts that would aid in determining whether appellee's conduct gave rise to a cause of action for violating appellant's civil rights.

Common Cause/Pennsylvania v. Commonwealth


NO. 795 M.D. 1997


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


710 A.2d 108; 1998 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 228


December 10, 1997, Argued  
March 31, 1998, Decided  
March 31, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended April 2, 1998.



DISPOSITION: The preliminary objections of Respondents and the Intervenors to Petitioners' petition for review are overruled in part and are sustained in part. Petitioners' petition for review is dismissed with prejudice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner citizens challenged the constitutionality of procedures followed by the General Assembly in enacting amendments to the Public Transportation Law and sought both injunctive and declaratory relief against respondent state officials. Respondents filed preliminary objections, asserting petitioners lacked standing, the matter was a non-justiciable, political question, and the claims failed to state a cause of action.


OVERVIEW: The General Assembly enacted substantial amendments to the Public Transportation Law which affected petitioner citizens. Petitioners challenged the constitutionality of the procedures followed by the General Assembly in enacting the amendments and sought injunctive and declaratory relief against respondent state officials. Respondents filed preliminary objections asserting petitioners lacked standing, the matter was a non-justiciable, political question, and failure to state a cause of action upon which relief could be granted. On review, the court observed that the action was not barred due to any lack of standing or that the claim was non-justiciable. However, the court found that petitioners were unable to prove facts legally sufficient to establish that the act was enacted in violation of Pa. Const. art. III, § 1. Specifically, the constitution was devoid of any procedural restrictions specifically relating to the enactment process for a transportation bill. Thus, the petition failed to state a claim upon which relief could be granted. Therefore, the preliminary objections were overruled in part and sustained in part and the petition for review was dismissed with prejudice.


OUTCOME: The preliminary objections of respondent state officials were overruled with respect to the claims of standing and justiciability but sustained with respect to the assertion that petitioner citizens complaint failed to state a cause of action and the petition for review was dismissed with prejudice. The court noted that there were no procedural restrictions specifically relating to the enactment process for the transportation bill at issue.

In re Puricelli


No. 1766 C.D. 1997


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


709 A.2d 1003; 1998 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 225


February 12, 1998, Argued  
March 27, 1998, Decided  
March 27, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 96-5355-19-5. Common Pleas Court of the County of Bucks. Judge SCOTT.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Bucks (Pennsylvania) that quashed appellant's land use challenge and awarded attorney's fees to appellee township.


OVERVIEW: Appellant purchased one of the lots of a proposed subdivision after tentative approval for development had been granted. The subdivision plan was then given final approval. Appellant challenged the deed restrictions imposed on further development of his lot. He then terminated the sales agreement into which he had entered. The trial court granted appellee township's motion to quash land use appeal and motion for attorney's fees, as appellant no longer had any interest in the property. The appeals court affirmed, holding that for purposes of pursuing his land use challenge, appellant did not have standing. Even if the court were to grant the relief he sought, it would be of no benefit to appellant since he no longer owned the land. The court held that the rule of collateral estoppel was not breached. The award of attorney's fees was warranted, as appellant offered no reason in defense of the court's determination that his continuing the land use appeal after rescinding the land sale agreement was vexatious and in bad faith.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the trial court that quashed appellant's land use challenge and awarded attorney's fees to appellee township, holding that appellant had no standing to obtain the relief sought because he lost interest in the property when he terminated the sales agreement. Attorney's fees were warranted because appellant acted in bad faith when he continued appeal of his land use.

 

477 of 892 DOCUMENTS


National Utils. v. Pennsylvania PUC


NO. 402 C.D. 1997


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


709 A.2d 972; 1998 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 160


February 11, 1998, Submitted on Briefs  
March 13, 1998, Decided  
March 13, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. R-00953416. State Agency, Public Utility Commission.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the PUC affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner public utility appealed an order of respondent Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, which adopted the recommended decision of the administrative law judge and denied petitioner's request to raise water rates to increase its annual operating revenues.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner public utility, owner and operator of 20 water utilities with 23 separate water systems, was denied a rate increase by respondent Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission pursuant to the Public Utility Code, 66 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 526 and 1501, for poor quality and quantity of water service. On appeal, petitioner claimed that respondent's action was confiscatory in violation of U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV because it did not allow for a reasonable rate of return on the value of petitioner's property. The court affirmed, holding that there was no violation of the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments when a public utility was denied an increase in rates for failure to provide adequate service to the public, even if the result was a rate of return that was less than the rate that petitioner would otherwise be entitled to receive. The court also held that respondent did not err in denying a rate increase by grouping all of petitioner's 23 separate water systems into one system, because rate increases were not granted based on individual systems but on divisions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of respondent Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission denying petitioner public utility's request for a rate increase. The court held that the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments were not violated when a public utility was denied an increase because it failed to provide adequate service to the public, even if the result was a rate of return less than petitioner would otherwise be entitled to receive.

 

478 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. CSX Transp.


No. 292 C.D. 1997


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


708 A.2d 138; 1998 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 150


November 3, 1997, Argued  
March 10, 1998, Decided  
March 10, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 3225 C 1991. Common Pleas Court of the County of Westmoreland. Judge MCCORMICK, JR.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commonwealth challenged an order of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Westmoreland (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee corporation's motion to quash the criminal information because prosecution would result in violation of Pa. Const. art. 1 and of U.S. Const. amend V.


OVERVIEW: Appellant commonwealth filed criminal charges against appellee corporation pursuant to the Fish and Boat Code, 30 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2504(a)(2), after an accident involving appellant's railway cars resulted in pollution and the destruction of wildlife. Appellee's first motion to quash the criminal information was granted by the trial court, but reversed by the court who held that § 2504(a)(2) did not violate the due process clauses of the federal and state constitutions. Appellee's motion to quash based upon double jeopardy was granted by the trial court, which held that appellee's penalty in the civil action, by the Department of Environmental Resources, was criminal in nature. The court reversed, holding that neither the U.S. Const. amend V or Pa. Const. art. 1, § 10 barred criminal prosecution where administrative proceedings of first action were civil in nature.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order granting appellee corporation's motion to quash criminal information because imposition of money sanctions did not subject appellee to double jeopardy under the Fifth Amendment of the U.S. Constitution and the Pennsylvania Constitution.

 

479 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Marrero by Tabales v. Commonwealth


No. 182 M.D. 1997


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


709 A.2d 956; 1998 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 144


September 10, 1997, Argued  
March 2, 1998, Decided  
March 2, 1998, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [**1] Respondents' preliminary objections, raising the issue of justiciability, sustained, and the instant petition for review with prejudice dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city, school district, and students, sought declaratory relief, asserting that respondent executive and legislative branches were required to appropriate more funds pursuant to the mandate contained in Pa. Const. art. III, § 14 as the local tax base did not generate the necessary funding to meet student needs. Respondents filed preliminary objections, raising the issue of justiciability, as well as numerous other contentions.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner city, school district, and students asserted that they were subjected to a financially over-burdened urban area with a disproportionately high tax burden. Petitioners filed a petition for review seeking declaratory relief arguing they were unable to rely on the local tax base to generate the necessary funding to meet the needs of students and that respondent legislative branch and executive branch were compelled to appropriate more funds pursuant to Pa. Const. art. III, § 14. Respondents thereafter filed preliminary objections, raising the issue of justiciability, along with many other arguments. On review, the court found that the issues involved were policy determinations that were clearly legislative, and not judicial in nature. The court determined that it was precluded from addressing the merits of the claims as the resolution of those issues were solely committed to the discretion of the legislature under Pa. Const. art. III, § 14. Therefore, respondents' preliminary objections raising the issue of justiciability were sustained and the court dismissed the petition with prejudice.


OUTCOME: The preliminary objections raised by respondents, legislative and executive branches, raising the issue of justiciability were sustained and the petition arguing reallocation of funds filed by petitioner city, school district, and students was dismissed with prejudice because the funding issue was a legislative function. This was because the issues involved were inherently policy determinations and not subject to the discretion of the judiciary.

In re Condemnation by DOT of Right of Way for Legislative Route 1021


No. 484 C.D. 1997, No. 1134 C.D. 1997


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


709 A.2d 939; 1998 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 103


November 5, 1997, Argued  
February 24, 1998, Decided  
February 24, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From. No. GD84-6940. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Judge LOUIK.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County is reversed as to the award for loss rentals and interest, and the order is affirmed as to the award for appraisal, attorney's and architectural fees stipulated to by counsel and for post-principal-litigation attorney's fees, court costs and expenses, real estate taxes, Airco, Inc. litigation expenses, insurance premiums and improvements to the condemned property. The case is remanded to the trial court to adjudicate the final attorney's fees due Condemnees consistent with the foregoing opinion. Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Pennsylvania Department of Transportation sought review of an order of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny (Pennsylvania), which granted a petition by appellees, owners of condemned property, and awarded damages under the Eminent Domain Code, 26 P.S. § 1-408.


OVERVIEW: Appellees, owners of condemned property, sought just compensation for the excessive taking of their property by appellant Pennsylvania Department of Transportation. The trial court awarded appellees their attorney's fees, costs and other damages arising out of the loss of a tenant of appellees' property pursuant to 26 P.S. § 1-408. The trial court concluded that § 408 required that just compensation mandated that appellees be made whole. Appellant sought review. The court reversed as to the award of lost rentals and interest, holding that just compensation requirements could not be applied in awarding statutory costs and expenses to a condemnee. The court affirmed as to the award for fees and expenses because under the clear and unambiguous language of § 408, appellees were entitled only to costs and expenses actually incurred. The court remanded the case for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed as to the award of damages to appellees, owners of condemned property, for loss rentals and interest, but affirmed the award with regard to fees and costs incurred. The statutory language of the Eminent Domain Code provided only for actual expenses incurred and not for damages under just compensation requirements. The case was remanded.

Wertz v. Chapman Twp.


No. 148 C.D. 1997


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


709 A.2d 428; 1998 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 101


September 10, 1997, Argued  
February 20, 1998, Decided  
February 20, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted August 26, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Pa. LEXIS 1855.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From. No. 793-94. Common Pleas Court of the County of Clinton. Judge SAXTON, President Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Court of Common Pleas of Clinton County, dated September 25, 1996, finding in favor of the defendants is vacated and the trial court is instructed to conduct a new trial. The order of the Court of Common Pleas of Clinton County, dated April 12, 1995, denying a jury trial is hereby affirmed. Jurisdiction is relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant road crew laborer sought review of a decision of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Clinton (Pennsylvania), which denied her motion for a jury trial of her sexual discrimination suit pursuant to the Pennsylvania Human Relations Act (PHRA) and found in favor of appellee township and supervisors.


OVERVIEW: Appellant road crew laborer alleged that her supervisor harassed her while she was pregnant and she filed a sexual discrimination suit against appellee township and supervisors. The matter ended up in state court where appellant filed a motion for a jury trial, which the trial court dismissed pursuant to the Pennsylvania Human Relations Act (PHRA). Thereafter the trial court found in favor of appellees. Appellant sought review and asserted that the trial court erred by excluding certain evidence, that the trial judge exhibited a bias in favor of appellees to warrant reassigning the case to a different judge, and that the PHRA created the right to demand a jury trial. On review, the court noted that the legislature was free to provide the right to a jury trial under the PHRA. However, pursuant to the language of the statute and the legislative history, the court found that there was no statutory or constitutional right to a jury trial. Therefore, the order denying a jury trial was affirmed. However, the trial court erred in evidentiary matters. Thus, the order finding in favor of appellees was vacated and the trial court was instructed to conduct a new trial.


OUTCOME: The order denying appellant road crew laborer a jury trial was affirmed because there was neither a statutory right under the Pennsylvania Human Relations Act nor a constitutional right to a jury trial in the sexual discrimination suit against appellee township and supervisors. However, the order in favor of appellees was vacated and the trial court was instructed to conduct a new trial because the trial court erred in evidentiary matters.

Nicoletti v. State Bd. of Vehicle Mfrs., Dealers & Salespersons


No. 1035 C.D. 1997


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


706 A.2d 891; 1998 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 50


November 5, 1997, Argued  
January 29, 1998, Decided  
January 29, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 92-60-03357 and 94-60-00835. State Board of Vehicle Manufacturers, Dealers and Salespersons.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, auto salesman and dealership, challenged an order from respondent State Board of Vehicle Manufacturers, Dealers and Salespersons (Pennsylvania) that revoked their licenses, pursuant to 63 P.S. § 818.19(4), after a criminal conviction. Appellant asserted that respondent lacked jurisdiction, that the statute was void for vagueness, and that the revocations violated double jeopardy and equal protection rights.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the order of respondent State Board of Vehicle Manufacturers, Dealers and Salespersons that revoked the licenses of appellants, auto salesperson and dealership, pursuant to 63 P.S. § 818.19(4). Appellant salesperson was convicted of conspiracy and mail fraud for his role in a scheme to defraud auto insurance companies. The court held that respondent had jurisdiction to revoke appellants' licenses despite the fact that those licenses were suspended or had not been renewed because appellants still had property interests in the licenses. The court held that the license revocation was a civil penalty that did not violate the Double Jeopardy Clause after appellant's conviction. The court held that the statute was not void for vagueness because respondent had promulgated regulations establishing the requirements for a license under the statute. Finally, the court held that there was no equal protection or due process violation for respondent's refusal to review the criminal backgrounds of every license holder in the state to determine if appellants were being treated unfairly.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed respondent board's order revoking appellant salesperson and dealership's licenses. The court held that respondent had jurisdiction despite the fact that the licenses were suspended, not renewed, and lapsed. The court held that civil penalty did not violate double jeopardy, that respondent's refusal to review all criminal backgrounds did not violate equal protection, and that regulations set standards for licenses.

 

483 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Royal Bank v. Commonwealth


NO. 758 F.R. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


705 A.2d 515; 1998 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 20


December 9, 1997, Argued  
January 8, 1998, Decided  
January 8, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. C-19,718. State Agency, Board of Finance and Revenue.



DISPOSITION: Appeal dismissed and order of the Board affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bank challenged an order from appellee Board of Finance and Revenue (Pennsylvania) that denied appellant's request for a refund of bank shares taxes paid for a single tax year. Appellant asserted that amendments to the Tax Reform Code, 72 P.S. § 7701, violated the Uniformity Clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1, because appellant, who had issued more shares and increased in value, was disproportionately affected.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed an order of appellee Board of Finance and Revenue that denied appellant bank's request for a refund of bank shares taxes it paid for tax year 1989. Following a U.S. Supreme Court decision that held that bank shares taxes computed on the basis of a bank's net assets had to deduct tax exempt U.S. obligations, in 1989 the legislature amended the Tax Reform Code, 72 P.S. § 7701, to provide for a one-time tax rate of 10.77 percent to make up for lost revenues. The court rejected appellant's assertion that the amendments violated the Uniformity Clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1, because it required appellant to pay a disproportionate share of the refund as a result of the fact that appellant had issued new shares since the time of the refund. The court held that there was no valid distinction that required appellee to treat appellant differently because it issued more shares or its value had increased. The court found that when the one-time tax rate stood on its own and was uniformly applied to all banks, whether one bank received a larger or smaller credit proportionate to what it paid was irrelevant in determining unconstitutionality.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellee board's order that refused appellant bank's request for a refund. The court held that the one-time tax rate did not violate the Uniformity Clause when it stood alone and was applied uniformly to all banks. The court stated that whether the tax rate caused a bank to receive a larger or smaller credit proportionate to what it paid was irrelevant.

 

484 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Peet v. Commonwealth


No. 596 F.R. 1994


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


705 A.2d 497; 1998 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 4


October 7, 1997, Argued  
January 2, 1998, Decided  
January 2, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 20315. State Agency, Board of Finance and Revenue.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is entered in favor of the Commonwealth. This order will become final, unless exceptions are filed within 30 days of the entry of this order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a husband and wife, sought review of an order of respondent Board of Finance and Revenue (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a decision denying petitioners a refund based on their personal income tax return.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, husband and wife, argued that respondent Department of Finance and Revenue erred in construing 72 P.S. § 7314(b), which provided petitioners a credit for "income subject to tax" by Delaware, because their entire gross income was used in the calculation of their Delaware tax liability and, thus, was "income subject to tax." Consequently, petitioners asserted, they were entitled a credit for the entire tax paid to Delaware and not just a percentage of the tax. The court disagreed, noting that Delaware used a taxpayer's total income, both in-state and out-of-state, to establish a tax rate and then applied that rate solely to the Delaware income. The court concluded that although out-of-state income was used by Delaware to calculate the appropriate tax rate, the out-of-state income itself was not subject to tax for purposes of § 7314(b). This provision was used by respondent to calculate the credit to which petitioners were entitled for income that was actually taxed by Delaware, rather than merely "considered" by Delaware in arriving at a tax rate. To rule otherwise could eliminate any tax liability in Pennsylvania, even though income was earned there.


OUTCOME: The decision denying petitioners, husband and wife, a refund based on their Pennsylvania personal income tax return was affirmed, because out-of-state income used by Delaware to set the tax rate on petitioners' Delaware earnings was not "income subject to tax" under Pennsylvania law that entitled petitioners to a Pennsylvania credit on all Delaware taxes paid.

Act I v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 3189 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


704 A.2d 732; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 912


September 11, 1997, Argued  
December 24, 1997, Decided  
December 24, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied July 13, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Pa. LEXIS 1388.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 1996-C-3327. Common Pleas Court of the County of Northampton. Judge MORAN.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Court of Common Pleas of Northampton County is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a property owner and a corporation licensed to operate group homes for youths, sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Northampton County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a decision of appellee township zoning hearing board denying appellants' request for permission to operate a group home for dependent female youths.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, a property owner and a licensed operator of group homes, argued that appellee township improperly denied their application to operate a group home for dependent girls. The court disagreed, ruling that the proposed use did not satisfy the requirements for a group home as established by an ordinance. Because this use did not involve any "handicapped" children, the proposed facility could not be considered a group home permitted by right, nor could the proposed use be permitted by a special exception as a use of the same character as a group home. The proposed living arrangement was simply not a use of the same character as a single family dwelling because the arrangement was of a transient nature and removed from the concept of a single family dwelling. Moreover, the maximum number of unrelated residents exceeded the number permitted by ordinance. Appellants were not entitled to a variance, because they failed to show that the property could not be used for purposes permitted by the ordinance. Finally, ordinance limitations on the number of unrelated persons living in a dwelling unit were constitutional, bearing a rational relationship to permissible social objectives.


OUTCOME: The order denying the application of appellants, a property owner and a licensed operator of group homes, to operate a girls' group home was affirmed; the proposed use did not satisfy an ordinance's "group home" requirements and was not of a character entitling appellants to a special exception, and the requisite "hardship" to obtain a variance was not shown. The ordinance limitation as to unrelated persons in a dwelling was constitutional.

Barbour-Knight v. Civil Serv. Comm'n


No. 1693 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


703 A.2d 572; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 873


October 6, 1997, Argued  
December 2, 1997, Decided  
December 2, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied July 16, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Pa. LEXIS 1400.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 95-10-SD-0090. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Judge LEVIN.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the decision of appellee city civil service commission sustaining appellant's dismissal from city employment pursuant to Philadelphia Home Rule Charter § 8-301.


OVERVIEW: Appellant argued that her dismissal from city employment pursuant to Philadelphia Home Rule Charter § 8-301 for holding dual government employment was improper because her second job, with the postal service (USPS), was not government employment within the meaning of § 8-301. The court disagreed, rejecting the contention that because USPS was an independent organization under the executive branch of government and independent of congressional control, employment with USPS was akin to working for a private employer. USPS was an "agency" of the federal government under the legal definition of the term, the court said, noting that the term encompassed independent federal organizations, Moreover, the Postal Reorganization Act (Act), 39 U.S.C.S. §§ 101-5605, in removing USPS from the political sphere, preserved for congress authority over USPS operations. As to appellant's argument that § 8-301 infringed her right to equal protection, in that no conflict of interest was involved, the court ruled that there was a basis for only allowing certain exceptions, as when special expertise was required, and that § 8-301 survived a rational basis review under a presumption of constitutionality.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed sustaining appellant's dismissal from city employment pursuant to the city's charter for holding dual government employment, because her second job, with the postal service, was government employment, as congress retained authority over its operations, and because the charter provision did not infringe appellant's equal protection rights, as the provision survived a rational basis review under a presumption of constitutionality.

COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

November 17, 1997, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 94-08532. Common Pleas Court of the County of Chester. Judge SUGERMAN.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order by the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania) which granted appellee's petition to strike a requested review by appellant of an earlier judgment entered in favor of appellee.


OVERVIEW: Appellee filed a complaint against appellant for unpaid sewer and refuse charges and for related late fees and penalties. Appellant filed a cross-complaint alleging that the late fees and penalties were usurious and unconstitutional. The trial court entered two separate judgments: one in favor of appellee on its complaint and the other in favor of appellee on appellant's cross-complaint. Appellant requested a single review of both issues. After reviewing Pa. R. Civ. P. D. J. 1004, the trial court concluded that appellant did not properly request a review. The court affirmed the denial of review because Rule 1004 required appellant to file two separate notices of review if he wished both adverse judgments entered against him to be reviewed. The court found that the rule clearly treated judgments entered on two cross-complaints as separate judgments. The court found that Pa. R. Civ. P. D. J. 315(C) did not require the trial court to enter a single judgment when there was a complaint and a cross-complaint. Furthermore, the court found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying review under Pa. R. Civ. P. 216, which allowed trial court to overlook procedural errors.


OUTCOME: Trial court's order denying review of two separate adverse judgments entered against appellant was affirmed because appellant failed to file two notices of review and trial court's refusal to overlook the procedural errors was not an abuse of discretion.

Parsowith v. Department of Revenue


No. 347 M.D. 1997


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 970


October 9, 1997, Argued  
November 7, 1997, Decided  
November 7, 1997, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [*1] The preliminary objections are sustained and the petition for review in this matter is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent state department of revenue (Pennsylvania) challenged by preliminary objections the petition for review brought by petitioner surviving spouse in original jurisdiction. Petitioner sought to have the court declare unconstitutional the tax rate under the Inheritance and Estate Tax Act, 72 P.S. §§ 9101 - 9196, for transfers of property to surviving spouses.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner surviving spouse paid an inheritance tax of six percent on settlements received in survival and wrongful death actions against various manufacturers of asbestos products. If her spouse had died in a later year, she would have paid no tax. Petitioner contended it was unconstitutional that the tax rate for transfers from decedents varied according to the year in which they died, under a phase-out of the tax on transfers to surviving spouses, under 72 P.S. § 9116(a)(1.1)(i), (ii). Respondent state department of revenue filed preliminary objections. The court held that petitioner had not exhausted adequate administrative remedies before she proceeded with constitutional claims. In essence, petitioner's action sought a refund for taxes paid and sought to avoid being taxed in the future. Under the Inheritance and Estate Tax Act, 72 P.S. §§ 9181, 9186, petitioner could seek a refund, pursue further judicial review if unsuccessful, protest an assessment, and appeal an agency determination. Petitioner showed no reason that she should have been excused from following statutory administrative procedure. The constitutional question alone did not confer equity jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court sustained respondent state department of revenue's preliminary objections and dismissed petitioner surviving spouse's request for review of the phase out of the inheritance tax rate because petitioner had failed to exhaust adequate administrative remedies before raising constitutional claims.

Stilp v. Hafer


NO. 336 M.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


701 A.2d 1387; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 971


September 9, 1997, Argued  
November 7, 1997, Decided  
November 7, 1997, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [**1] 1. Petitioners' motion for peremptory judgment is denied; and 2. Respondents' motion for summary judgment is granted and judgment is entered in favor of respondents and against petitioners.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners individuals opposed the summary judgment motion of respondents, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania and its governor and treasurer, that asserted that petitioners' complaint that sought to invalidate the Low Level Radioactive Waste Disposal Act, 35 P.S. §§7130.101 - 7130.905, as unconstitutional under the Pa. Const. art. III, §§1, 2, 4 was barred by laches.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners individuals filed a complaint that alleged that the procedural process used to enact the Low Level Radioactive Waste Disposal Act, 35 P.S. §§7130.101 - 7130.905, violated Pa. Const. art. III, §§1, 2, 4. Respondents Commonwealth of Pennsylvania and its governor and treasurer filed a summary judgment motion and alleged that the matter was barred by laches. The court held that because petitioners' challenge to the legislation was on procedural grounds after its passage, the laches doctrine if proven was applicable to the constitutional challenge. Petitioners argued that laches was not a proper subject for a summary judgment motion because the doctrine was factual in nature, but the court held that it was proper on the face of the pleadings where petitioners failed to file a timely response to respondents' summary judgment motion pursuant to Pa. R. Civ. P. 1035.5. Thus, the court granted respondents' motion because petitioners did not act with due diligence in filing the complaint eight years after the statute's enactment and because the delay caused respondents to suffer prejudice that included substantial costs implementing the legislation.


OUTCOME: The court granted the summary judgment motion of respondents, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania and its governor and treasurer, and held that petitioners' complaint that sought to invalidate a state statute as unconstitutional was barred by laches. Petitioners were denied relief because laches was applicable on respondents' summary judgment motion and its elements established that petitioners were barred from relief.

Hill v. Workmen's Compensation Appeal Bd. (Spirit of Philadelphia)


No. 1376 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


703 A.2d 74; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 790; 1998 AMC 351


October 9, 1996, Argued  
October 30, 1997, Decided  
October 30, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied April 24, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Pa. LEXIS 848.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. A94-1497. State Agency, Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner claimant challenged an order of respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board (Pennsylvania) to dismiss his claim for workmen's compensation benefits under the Pennsylvania Workers' Compensation Act, 77 P.S. §1 et seq., for lack of subject matter jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner claimant was a deckhand on a tour boat that plied the navigable waters of the Delaware River. Petitioner asserted that he was injured while he was performing one of his duties as an employee, which was making a trash run to an offshore dumpster. Respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board dismissed the petition for lack of jurisdiction under the Pennsylvania Workers' Compensation Act (PWCA), 77 P.S. §1 et seq., because petitioner failed to prove that his duties were sufficiently land based. Petitioner asserted that because his injury occurred within the territorial borders of Pennsylvania he was entitled to benefits under the PWCA. The court held that because petitioner was a seaman and his injury occurred in the course of his employment his state claim was pre-empted by federal law, in particular under the Jones Act, 46 U.S.C.S. §688 et seq. The Jones Act had a very liberal definition of a seaman, which included petitioner's status as one who had a more or less permanent connection to a vessel. Also, since petitioner was working pursuant to his employer's orders he was acting within the course of his employment, even though the injury occurred on land.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board that dismissed petitioner claimant's state cause of action for workmen's compensation benefits, because respondent properly determined that it lacked subject matter jurisdiction in the matter.

Philadelphia Fraternal Order of Correctional Officers v. Rendell


NO. 1070 M.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


701 A.2d 600; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 410


May 7, 1997, Argued  
October 3, 1997, Decided  
October 3, 1997, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [**1] Motion for summary relief granted and the entire action dismissed. Petitioners' cross-motion for summary relief denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner correctional officers sought review of an order of respondent Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board which dismissed petitioner's action. Petitioners also filed an original action against respondents, the City of Philadelphia and the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, challenging the constitutionality of an ordinance of respondent city and 43 P.S. §1101.2003, in an action involving petitioners' choice of a bargaining representative.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner correctional officers formed an organization after experiencing dissatisfaction with the collective bargaining unit assigned to them under Philadelphia, Pa., Ordinance of April 4, 1961 (1961 Ordinance). Respondent Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board (PLRB) dismissed petitioners' action requesting that respondent City of Philadelphia be required to recognize their chosen bargaining unit. Petitioners contended that respondent city's refusal to recognize their chosen bargaining unit infringed on their rights of association and equal protection. The court affirmed the order of respondent PLRB and the order of summary relief in favor of respondents, dismissed petitioners' complaint, and denied their cross-motion for summary relief. The 1961 Ordinance, saved by 43 P.S. §1101.2003, acted as an absolute bar on petitioners' rights to choose a labor organization for the purposes of collective bargaining. U.S. Const. amend I and Pa. Const. art. I, § 7 allowed workers to voice their views, but did not require employers to engage in collective bargaining. Moreover, respondent city safeguarded its legitimate interest in ensuring it would not be overburdened with labor matters.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of respondent Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board which dismissed petitioners' action. Petitioners' original action involving their choice of a bargaining representative was dismissed and summary judgment was granted in favor of respondents city and Commonwealth. Respondent city had a legitimate interest in not recognizing petitioners' chosen bargaining unit, and one was already assigned by ordinance.

Golden Triangle News v. Corbett


No. 1161 M.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


700 A.2d 1056; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 396


March 11, 1997, Argued  
September 12, 1997, Decided  
September 12, 1997, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [**1] The preliminary objections of respondents, Hon. Thomas Corbett and Col. Paul Evanko, asserting that this court lacks jurisdiction over them are denied. The remainder of respondents' preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer, including those of Hon. Thomas Corbett and Col. Paul Evanko, are granted, and petitioners' amended petition for review is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought summary judgment against respondents, state and county attorneys general, city and township solicitors, state police commissioner, and city, who cross-moved for summary judgment and demurred in an action for declaratory and injunctive relief under adult oriented establishment restriction statute, Act of 1996, 68 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5501-5509.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners sought a declaration of unconstitutionality of Act of 1996, Pa. Const. Stat. §§ 5501-5509, and injunctive relief enjoining enforcement by respondents, state and city attorneys general, state police commissioner, and city and township solicitors. The court denied petitioners' summary judgment motion and dismissed respondents' summary judgment motion as moot. The court found petitioners failed to establish entitlement to the requested relief when the act which restricted adult entertainment establishments was not an impermissible content based free speech restriction where it was aimed at ameliorating secondary effects of adult entertainment, as opposed to the suppression of free speech itself. The court noted, as a result, that the regulation was considered content neutral, and the standard to be applied was analyzing whether the regulation was narrowly tailored to serve a significant governmental interest while leaving open ample alternative channels of communication. The court further explained governmental attempts to reduce adverse secondary effects of adult entertainment was recognized as furthering the substantial interest in protecting citizen heath and welfare.


OUTCOME: Court denied petitioners' summary judgment motion and dismissed as moot respondents', attorneys general, police commissioner, and solicitors, summary judgment motion because entitlement to declaratory and injunctive relief was lacking when statute regulating adult entertainment was not an impermissible content based free speech restriction, but instead content neutral aimed at legitimate interest of reducing secondary effects of such industry.

 

494 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Stilp v. Commonwealth


No. 1 M.D. 1997


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


699 A.2d 1353; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 372


May 5, 1997, Submitted On Briefs  
August 20, 1997, Decided  
August 20, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Stilp v. Commonwealth, 910 A.2d 775, 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 604 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2006)



DISPOSITION:  [**1] The preliminary objection in the nature of a demurrer of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Governor Thomas J. Ridge and Treasurer Catherine Baker Knoll is hereby sustained and the Complaint in Mandamus and Bill of Equity of Gene Stilp is hereby dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent public officials filed preliminary objection in the nature of a demurrer to petitioner citizen's complaint that an amendment to the Public Official Compensation Law, 65 P.S. §§ 366.1-366.5b, which provided for a pay increase for members of the legislature, was unconstitutional under Pa. Const. art. II, § 8. This matter was before the court in its original jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: The legislature passed an act amending the Public Official Compensation Law, 65 P.S. §§ 366.1-366.5b, which provided for a cost of living adjustment (COLA) salary increase and unvouchered expense allowance increase for legislators. Petitioner citizen contended the act violated Pa. Const. art. II, § 8 because it would allow incumbent legislators to receive the new salary over the next succeeding term and because the expense allowance increase amounted to an immediately-effective pay increase. Respondent public officials filed preliminary objection in the nature of a demurrer. The court sustained respondents' preliminary objection and dismissed petitioner's complaint. The expense allowance was an amount paid to cover expenses incurred in the performance of the legislators' services; thus, even though the allowance increased under a COLA formula, it was not a salary subject to Pa. Const. art. II, § 8. Petitioner did not allege the allowance was unneeded. The act that effected the pay increase contained a provision that it was to take effect immediately and "be applicable as constitutionally permissible." Thus, the act was by its own terms not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court sustained respondent public officials' preliminary objection in the nature of a demurrer because petitioner citizen had failed to state a cause of action. The expense allowance and pay increase approved by the legislature was not unconstitutional. The court dismissed petitioner's complaint.

 

495 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Holsten v. Haverford Twp.


No. 32 C.D. 1997


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


698 A.2d 174; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 342


June 20, 1997, Submitted On Briefs  
July 31, 1997, Decided  
July 31, 1997, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. S.A. 40555-96. Common Pleas Court of the County of Delaware. Judge MORGAN, SR., Senior Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Delaware County Court of Common Pleas, No. SA 40555-96, dated October 30, 1996, is hereby affirmed in part and reversed in part. The order is affirmed with respect to Holsten's conviction for violating § 49-7 of the ordinance on March 20, 1996 (Citation No. A126505); the order is reversed with respect to Holsten's conviction for violating § 49-12(A) on January 30, 1996 (Citation No. A126492); and the order is reversed with respect to her conviction for violating § 49-7 on September 28, 1995 (Citation A126478). Further, we remand the case to the court of common pleas for a modification, if any, of the fine and court costs assessed on Citation No. A126478. Jurisdiction Relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant pet owner challenged her conviction by the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania) for letting her dogs and rabbit run at large under Haverford Township, Pa., Ord. 1806, art. I., §§ 49-7 and 49-12, respectively.


OVERVIEW: Appellant pet owner was convicted of letting her dogs run at large on two different occasions under Haverford Township, Pa., Ord. 1806, art. I, § 49-7, and of allowing her rabbit to trespass on her neighbor's property under § 49-12. Appellant sought review. The trial court noted that it had erred in convicting appellant of the violation of § 49-12 because the rabbit had not committed any damage, as required under the ordinance. Consequently, the court reversed that conviction. The court also reversed appellant's first conviction for allowing her dogs to run at large because it had not been shown that, on that day, the dogs had ever left appellant's property or posed any threat to the public. To avoid having to find § 49-7 unconstitutional, the court interpreted it to require one of those two elements, although it did not interpret the ordinance to require any criminal intent or guilty knowledge. The second violation of § 49-7 was affirmed, where the dogs were found at a different address. Finally, the court remanded for any necessary modification of the fines imposed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed appellant pet owner's first conviction for allowing her dogs to run at large because the dogs had not left appellant's property. The court affirmed the second conviction because the dogs were found elsewhere. The court also reversed the conviction for allowing the rabbit to escape, finding that it had not damaged any property. Finally, the court remanded for a modification, if any, of the fines and court costs assessed.

Travaglia v. Department of Corrections


No. 2473 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


699 A.2d 1317; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 338


March 10, 1997, Argued  
July 29, 1997, Decided  
July 29, 1997, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal and Cross-Petition Denied December 23, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Pa. LEXIS 2738.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From Denial Dated August 1, 1996. State Agency Department of Corrections.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner death row inmate appealed from a determination of respondent Department of Corrections (Pennsylvania), denying him access to certain information relative to its use of lethal injections for carrying out death sentences on the ground that the information was not subject to disclosure under 65 P.S. §§ 66.2 and 66.3, provisions of the state Right-to-Know Act.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner death row inmate requested information as to the use of lethal injections to carrying out death sentences from respondent, Department of Corrections, claiming to be entitled to the information under the state's Right-to-Know Act. After respondent denied his request, petitioner appealed. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. After rejecting a challenge to petitioner's standing to appeal, the court found that some of the requested materials were "public records" within the meaning of 65 P.S. § 66.1(2). The court held that petitioner was entitled to materials generally concerning the injection procedure, as well as information concerning the identity and recorded observations of execution witnesses. However, the court upheld respondent's refusal to produce records pertaining to actual methods of injection and related security measures because a risk to the public might result. The court also found that while photographs of prior executions were outside the ambit of the statute, and thus were not subject to disclosure, certain medical reports such as post-mortem reports were subject to disclosure.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the denial of requests by petitioner death row inmate for data as to the specific manner in which executions by lethal injection were carried out, as well as for photographs of prior executions. The court required disclosure of information as to the identity of witnesses to prior executions, their recorded observations, and general information concerning the injection procedure.

Stranahan v. County of Mercer


No. 2871 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


697 A.2d 1049; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 304


June 2, 1997, Argued  
July 7, 1997, Decided  
July 7, 1997, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied August 29, 2000, Reported at: 2000 Pa. LEXIS 2159.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 1996-1545. Common Pleas Court of the County of Mercer. Judge SMITH, Senior Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Court of Common Pleas of Mercer County is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of the judgment of the Common Pleas Court of the of County of Mercer (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee county's preliminary objections and dismissed appellants' complaint in their class action for individual tax refunds pursuant to 72 P.S. §§ 5566(b), (c).


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayers challenged the judgment that sustained appellee county's preliminary objections and dismissed appellants' compliant in their class action for individual tax refunds. Appellants challenged the constitutionality of the County Personal Property Tax Act, 72 P.S. §§ 4821-4902 in a class action. The court affirmed the judgment and held that a class action complaint could not be used to secure individual tax refunds. The court held that the legislature provided statutory procedures for appellants to obtain a refund of erroneously paid taxes under 72 P.S. §§ 5566b and 5566c. The court held that those procedures had to be narrowly construed and required the aggrieved individual to file for a refund before bringing an action in assumpsit. Pursuant to § 5566b, appellants were required to first request a refund. The court held that class actions could not be utilized to obtain individual tax refunds because the right to a refund belonged only to the aggrieved individual and a specific statutory remedy was available.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because the right to a tax refund belonged only to the aggrieved individual and appellant taxpayers' class action could not be utilized to obtain individual tax refunds.

Pennsylvania Sch. Bds. Ass'n v. Commonwealth Ass'n of Sch. Adm'rs, Teamsters Local 502


No. 1129 M.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


696 A.2d 859; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 295


April 11, 1997, Argued  
July 2, 1997, Decided  
July 2, 1997, FILED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner school administrators filed complaint against respondents labor union, and state officials, alleging that the collective bargaining provisions of 71 P.S. § 371(1) violated the state constitution and the statutory provisions failed to comply with procedures for obtaining approval of the state budget. Respondents filed preliminary objections to the petition for review asserting that the court lacked subject matter jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner school administrators filed a five-count petition for review. Counts I, II, IV and V of the petition alleged that the collective bargaining and arbitration provisions of 71 P.S. § 371(1) violated the state constitution. Count III of the petition alleged that because the statute failed to comply with procedures for obtaining approval of the state budget, respondent governor violated his constitutional duties to faithfully execute the laws and to return any bill of that he did not approve when he signed the statute. Respondent union and state officials objected. The court held that because none of the state respondents was an indispensable party to Counts I, II, IV or V of the petition, the court lacked original jurisdiction over those counts under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 761(a). Accordingly, the court transferred those counts to the lower trial court for further proceedings. The court concluded that the claims asserted in Count III were non-justiciable under the Enrolled Bill Doctrine, and that count was dismissed as to all parties with prejudice. Respondent union's objections with respect to standing were overruled, but sustained as to subject matter jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the preliminary objections of respondents as to subject matter jurisdiction with respect to Counts I, II, IV and V of the petition for review. The court sustained the preliminary objections filed by the state respondents with respect to Count III on the grounds that the claims asserted therein were non-justiciable, and that count was dismissed with prejudice. The remaining counts were transferred to a trial court.

E. Lampeter Twp. v. County of Lancaster

COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 5178-1996. Common Pleas Court of the County of Lancaster. Judge ALLISON.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County dated January 17, 1997, is hereby reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township sought review of the order of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Lancaster (Pennsylvania), which granted the preliminary objections of appellees, county, county human relations commission, and landowner, in appellant's action under the Declaratory Judgments Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7531 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellee landowner filed a complaint with appellee county human relations commission, alleging that appellant township violated Lancaster County, Pa., Ordinance No. 30 by denying his petition to rezone a tract of land based on his national origin. Appellant filed a motion to dismiss, contending that appellee commission had no jurisdiction. The motion was denied. DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district challenged a decision the Court of Common Pleas of the 26th Judicial District, Montour County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an arbitrator's order and held that 24 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8302 could be applied retroactively to increase an employee's pension benefit.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to employee's retirement, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8302(b.2) was passed. The new legislation allowed employee to increase her pension benefit. Employee sought the increase and appellant school district denied her request. Pursuant to a collective bargaining agreement, the matter was submitted to arbitration. The arbitrator granted employee's request for a pension increase, and the trial court affirmed the arbitrator's order. The court also held that retroactive application of the statute violated the contracts clause of the United States and Pennsylvania Constitutions. The court reasoned that § 8302(b.2) impaired the rights and obligations of appellant under the collective bargaining agreement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the arbitrator's order that retroactively increased an employee's pension benefits, holding that the retroactive application of the statute at issue violated the contracts clause of the United States and Pennsylvania Constitutions.

502 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Bergdoll v. Kane


No. 516 M.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


694 A.2d 1155; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 225


November 20, 1996, Argued  
May 19, 1997, Decided  
May 19, 1997, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [**1] Petitioners motion for summary judgment granted, and Secretary's motion for summary judgment denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff attorneys, taxpayers, and electors and defendant commonwealth brought cross motions for summary judgment before the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) in plaintiff's action in quo warranto to enjoin defendant from placing proposed constitutional amendments on the ballot. The parties disputed plaintiffs' standing to challenge the constitutionality of the amendment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff attorneys, taxpayers, and electors brought a complaint in quo warranto to enjoin the commonwealth from placing on the ballot a proposed constitutional amendment regarding an accused's right to confront witnesses and enactment of laws on procedures governing the testimony of children in criminal proceedings. In their cross motions for summary judgment after a majority of the electorate voted in favor of the ballot question, the parties disputed plaintiffs' standing to challenge the constitutionality of the amendment. The court noted that plaintiffs were sworn to defend the constitution and that they and their clients were directly impacted by the amendment. Since plaintiffs' interest in the matter was substantial, direct, and immediate, the court concluded that they had standing. The court also declared the ballot question null and void because it proposed two amendments to the constitution with a single question. Accordingly, plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment was granted and defendant's motion for summary judgment was denied.


OUTCOME: The summary judgment motion of plaintiff attorneys, taxpayers, and electors was granted and defendant commonwealth's motion for summary judgment was denied. Plaintiffs were sworn to defend the constitution and their clients would be impacted by the amendment, so their standing was therefore established by the substantial, direct, and immediate interest they had in the matter.

 

503 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Haller v. Commonwealth


NO. 241 M.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


693 A.2d 266; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 200


November 15, 1995, ARGUED  
May 1, 1997, Decided  
May 1, 1997, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [**1] Motion for summary judgment of Felice Newman and Steve Zupcic granted. The department's motion for summary judgment denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, publisher and consumer, filed an action and moved for summary judgment against respondents, Pennsylvania Department of Revenue and its Director. Respondents filed a cross-motion for summary judgment. The parties' dispute was over whether the exemption from state sales tax of Bibles and other religious publications violated U.S. Const. amend. I, and Pa. Const. art. 1, § 3.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, publisher and consumer, filed an action against respondents, Pennsylvania Department of Revenue and its Director, that challenged the federal and state constitutionality of the state statute that exempted from state sales tax Bibles and other religious materials. On stipulated facts the parties moved for summary judgment and the court granted petitioners' motion. The court found that the tax exemption violated both U.S. Const. amend. I, and Pa. Const. art. 1, § 3. The court noted that the United States Supreme Court had already issued decisions to the same effect. The court distinguished the exemption from the real estate tax exemption enjoyed by religious organizations. The court noted that the real estate exemption had existed since the nation's founding and that the same exemption applied to hospitals and other charitable organizations. The court held that while imposing real estate taxes on religious organizations would likely increase an entanglement between the secular government and religion, for example because of potential foreclosures, the imposition of the sales tax on religious publications would not interfere with anyone's practice of religion.


OUTCOME: On stipulated facts the court granted the motion for summary judgment of petitioners, publisher and consumer. The court ruled that the exemption from sales tax of religious publications violated both the federal and state constitutions. The court ruled that there was no proof that imposition of the tax would abridge anyone's right to practice their religion.

Zangrilli v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 2848 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


692 A.2d 656; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 184


March 10, 1997, Argued  
April 16, 1997, Decided  
April 16, 1997, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied August 19, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Pa. LEXIS 1672.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. S.A. 2968-95. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Judge McLEAN.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, dated September 25, 1996, at No. S.A. 2968-95, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners challenged the decision of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny (Pennsylvania), which found in favor of respondent zoning board and which held that zoning ordinance that prohibited "repair of motor vehicles" in an R-2 district was valid.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowners owned a residential dwelling in an R-2 residential district. A detached two-car garage was located on the property. Respondent borough served an enforcement notice upon appellants to stop all auto repair maintenance work in the garage. Appellants filed an appeal, respondent zoning board denied appellants' claim, and the trial court affirmed. On review, appellants argued that the ordinance discriminated between carport and private parking lot owners and those who owned private garages. However, the court found that appellants failed to provide evidence that they were treated differently than other residents in the R-2 district. Furthermore, the court found that a garage in which major vehicle repairs were performed could produce noise, fumes, and dirt which would disturb the residential character of the area. Prevention of such interferences was a legitimate governmental interest and was not achieved in an arbitrary or unreasonable manner. The court also rejected appellants' argument that the phrase "repair of motor vehicles" was vague because residents could understand that simple auto repairs would be acceptable. The trial court's decision was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The trial court's decision that found in favor of respondent zoning board and that held that zoning ordinance was valid was affirmed. The court concluded that there was no violation of due process because the ordinance was aimed at a legitimate governmental interest and that the ordinance was neither arbitrary nor unreasonable.

Gombach v. Department of State, Bureau of Comm'ns, Elections & Legislation


No. 1913 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


692 A.2d 1127; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 171


March 13, 1997, Argued  
April 14, 1997, Decided  
April 14, 1997, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Name of this Case has been Corrected by the Court April 16, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 0002-NOT-96. State Agency Department of State.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner notary public challenged the decision of respondent, the Department of State, Bureau of Commissions, Elections, & Legislation (Pennsylvania), which denied petitioner's application to be recommissioned as a notary public on the ground that petitioner failed to establish that he was of good moral character.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner notary public's application to be recommissioned as a notary public was denied by respondent state agency on the ground that petitioner was convicted of income tax evasion and thus failed to establish that he was of good moral character. A hearing was held wherein petitioner's application was formally rejected. On appeal, petitioner argued that the requirement for a notary to be of good moral character was constitutionally vague, thereby violating his right to due process. However, the court found that procedural due process required that petitioner be given adequate information with which to prepare a defense. Furthermore, petitioner was fully informed of the charges against him and the statutory section involved. Therefore, petitioner received the minimum protection required under due process of law. Finally, petitioner's argument that it was cruel and unusual punishment for respondent to revoke his notary commission for an unspecified period was without merit because respondent was not required to provide petitioner with a date certain. Accordingly, respondent's order was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Respondent state agency's decision that denied petitioner notary public's application to be recommissioned as a notary public was affirmed because petitioner failed to establish that he was of good moral character. Furthermore, the term "good moral character" was not unconstitutionally vague because petitioner was given adequate information regarding his violation with which to prepare a defense.

Pennsylvania State Lodge, FOP v. Commonwealth

DISPOSITION:  [**1] The preliminary objections of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, and Department of Labor and Industry to the petition for review in the nature of an action for declaratory judgment filed by the Pennsylvania State Lodge, Fraternal Order of Police, are sustained, and the petition for review in the nature of an action for declaratory judgment is dismissed with prejudice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner police union brought an action for declaratory relief against respondent Department of Labor and Industry challenging the constitution of certain recently enacted statutes.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner police union sought declaratory relief against respondent Department of Labor and Industry. Petitioner challenged the constitutionality of the amendments to the Workers' Compensation Act as contained in sections 3 and 22 of Act 57 of 1996. Petitioner argued that Act 57 unconstitutionally distinguished between Act 111 employees and other public and private employees. The court denied the requested relief, holding that Pa. Const. art. III, § 31 specifically acknowledges a fundamental distinction between the collective bargaining rights of policemen and firemen and all other employees, and the settlement of disputes between policemen and firemen and their public employers. The court thus held that the statutory distinction drawn between Act 111 employees and all other employees in Act 57 of 1996 did not violate Pa. Const. art. III, § 32.


OUTCOME: The court denied the requested declaratory relief of petitioner police union, holding that the statutory amendments did not violate the Pennsylvania Constitution.

League of Women Voters v. Commonwealth


No. 711 M.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


692 A.2d 263; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 133


December 11, 1996, ARGUED  
April 1, 1997, Decided  
April 1, 1997, FILED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners requested from the court a declaration that the General Appropriations Act of 1996 was unconstitutionally or unlawfully enacted or, in the alternative, that those portions of the Act which were inserted by a conference committee were unconstitutionally enacted. They further requested that respondents be enjoined from performing the provisions of the Act. Respondents filed preliminary objections to the amended petition for review.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners alleged that the General Assembly violated mandatory provisions of the Pennsylvania Constitution when it enacted the General Appropriations Act of 1996 (Act) because a committee of conference purported to introduce changes to individual line item appropriations that went beyond the scope of disagreement between the two chambers of the General Assembly. Petitioners requested that respondents be enjoined from performing the provisions of the Act. The court concluded that petitioner had standing to raise their constitutional claims. However, the court concluded that judicial scrutiny of petitioners' constitutional claims was barred by the Enrolled Bill Doctrine, which provided that when a law had been passed and approved and certified in due form, it was no part of the duty of the judiciary to go behind the law as duly certified to inquire into the observance of form in its passage. Further, the court held that the Enrolled Bill Doctrine precluded it from considering whether the conference committee violated the Sunshine Act, 65 P.S. §§ 271-286. The court dismissed petitioners' amended petition for review with prejudice.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed petitioners' amended petition for review with prejudice. Judicial scrutiny of petitioners' claim that the General Assembly violated mandatory provisions of the Pennsylvania Constitution when it enacted the General Appropriations Act of 1996 was barred by the Enrolled Bill Doctrine.
