Pennsylvania AFL-CIO by George v. Commonwealth


No. 704 M.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


691 A.2d 1023; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 134


December 11, 1996, Argued  
April 1, 1997, Decided  
April 1, 1997, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [**1] Petitioners' petition for review and the Belan petition for review are dismissed with prejudice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners and intervenors, a labor union, state legislators, and groups, filed petitions for review seeking a declaratory judgment that a statute was unconstitutionally or unlawfully enacted as well as injunctive relief. Respondent state government and officials filed preliminary objections, claiming judicial review was barred by various constitutional doctrines.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners and intervenors, a labor union, state legislators, and two groups representing injured workers, filed petitions for review in the nature of complaints in equity addressed to the court's original jurisdiction. Petitioners sought to permanently enjoin respondents, a state government and its officials, from implementing the provisions of a state statute modifying the workers' compensation procedure. Petitioners requested a declaratory judgment that the statute was unconstitutionally or unlawfully enacted. Respondents and another group of intervenor state legislators, who supported respondents, filed preliminary objections to the petition for review, contending that judicial inquiry into the claims of petitioners and supporting intervenors was barred by the Enrolled Bill Doctrine, the Political Question Doctrine, and the Speech and Debate Clause of the state constitution. While finding that the claims of petitioners and intervenors were not barred, the court analyzed the procedure used in the passage of the statute and determined that it complied with the constitutional mandates of Pa. Const. art. III, §§ 1-5. Thus, the petitions for review were dismissed with prejudice.


OUTCOME: The preliminary objections of respondents and its intervenors to petitioners' petition for review were overruled with respect to the issues of justiciability, but were sustained with respect to all other issues. The court concluded that petitioners and their intervenors had failed to prove facts legally sufficient to establish that the statute was unconstitutionally or unlawfully enacted so as to overcome the presumption of constitutionality.

Beardsley v. State Emples. Retirement Bd.


No. 465 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


691 A.2d 1016; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 132


December 11, 1996, Argued  
March 31, 1997, Decided  
March 31, 1997, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 1992-11 (a-mm). State Agency State Employes' Retirement Board.



DISPOSITION: The Board's decision is reversed with regard to EMIP payments and Reeher's application and affirmed with regard to annual leave payments.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner management employees appealed an order from the State Employees' Retirement Board (Pennsylvania), which determined that marketing incentive payments were not compensation under the Pennsylvania State Employees' Retirement Code, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5101 - 5956.


OVERVIEW: Respondent State Employees' Retirement Board determined that payments made to petitioner management employees pursuant to a early retirement incentive program were not compensation for retirement purposes under the Pennsylvania State Employee's Retirement Code, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5101 - 5956. Petitioners appealed and the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. Thc court found that respondent's findings of fact were supported by the evidence. The court held that the payments were not bonuses, but incentive payments included as compensation under 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5102 because they were intended as compensation, represented a substantial percentage of the participants' total salaries, had been paid regularly over a period of years, and had specific standards for payment. The court found that annual leave was excluded from compensation under § 5102. One petitioner's conditional retirement application should have been accepted because the accommodation offered was illusory.


OUTCOME: The court reversed respondent State Employees' Retirement Board's decision finding the incentive payments were not compensation, reversed the denial of one petitioner management employee's retirement application, and affirmed respondent's holding that unused annual leave was not compensation. The court found that the incentive payments were not bonuses and were included as compensation for retirement purposes.

Ridge v. State Employees' Retirement Bd.


No. 545 M.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


690 A.2d 1312; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 148; 71 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P44,868


November 20, 1996, ARGUED  
March 13, 1997, Decided  
March 13, 1997, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [**1] Petitioner's motion for judgment on the pleadings is denied. The Respondent's motion for leave to amend its new matter to assert a statute of limitations defense is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner retired judge filed a motion for judgment on the pleadings in his original action in mandamus and for declaratory judgment, alleging that the use by respondent, State Employees' Retirement Board of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, of gender-based actuarial tables in calculating retirement benefits for credited service was unlawful. Respondent's motion for leave to amend its answer was also before the court.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner retired judge filed an action for a writ of mandamus seeking an order for respondent, State Employees' Retirement Board of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, to recalculate his retirement benefits using gender-neutral actuarial tables. Petitioner also sought a declaration that respondent's use of gender-based actuarial tables in calculating retirement benefits for credited service violated Pa. Const. art. 1, §§ 26 and 28. Respondent filed its answer and a motion to amend asserting a statute of limitations defense. Petitioner filed a motion for judgment on the pleadings and on his application for a writ of mandamus, both of which the court denied. Petitioner failed to demonstrate that he was entitled to judgment on the pleadings, or that he had a clear right to relief. The court found that it did not need to reach the constitutionality of how petitioner's benefits were calculated as petitioner failed to establish that his recalculated benefits would have been retroactively applied. As petitioner did not show that he was prejudiced from respondent's delay in raising of the statute of limitations defense, the court granted respondent's motion for leave to amend.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner retired judge's motion for judgment on the pleadings in his mandamus and declaratory judgment action against respondent State Employees' Retirement Board of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania. Petitioner did not show that he was entitled to judgment. The court did not consider whether petitioner's retirement benefits were lawfully calculated. The court granted respondent's motion to amend.

Mazza v. DOT, Bureau of Driver Licensing


NO. 3464 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


692 A.2d 251; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 154


November 20, 1996, Submitted on Briefs  
March 13, 1997, Decided  
March 13, 1997, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied March 31, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Pa. LEXIS 595.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed from No. 4370-S-1995. Common Pleas Court of the County of Dauphin. Judge LIPSITT, Senior Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County at No. 4370 § 1995, dated November 21, 1995, is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver requested review from a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania) that sustained the suspension of his operating privileges for one year under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1532(b)(3) by appellee, Pennsylvania Department of Transportation. Appellant contended that the suspension was improperly based on an out-of-state conviction for driving under the influence of alcohol.


OVERVIEW: The trial court affirmed a decision by appellee, Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, suspending appellant driver's operating privileges for a year pursuant to 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1532(b)(3). Appellant was convicted of driving under the influence of alcohol and had another conviction in New Jersey for the same offense. Appellant contended that the suspension was improper, because he would have been eligible for the accelerated rehabilitation program if his first conviction was in the commonwealth instead of New Jersey. The court did not address appellant's argument. However, the court reversed the order dismissing the appeal. The court held that the legislature did not authorize appellee to consider out out-of-state convictions, and respondent did not have jurisdiction to suspend appellant's license based on the New Jersey conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order sustaining the suspension of appellant driver's operating privileges by appellee, Pennsylvania Department of Transportation. Appellee was not authorized to consider appellant's out-of-state conviction for operating under the influence of alcohol when suspending appellant's driving privileges.

Appeal granted by, Sub nomine at PPG Indus. v. Pennsylvania Ins. Dep't, 555 Pa. 748, 725 A.2d 1224, 1998 Pa. LEXIS 1539 (1998)
Reversed by LaFarge Corp. v. Insurance Dep't, 557 Pa. 544, 735 A.2d 74, 1999 Pa. LEXIS 2067 (1999)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. MS95-10-056. State Agency Department of Insurance.



DISPOSITION: 1. The order of the Insurance Commissioner of Pennsylvania at No. MS95-10-056, dated February 7, 1996, is vacated. 2. The matter is remanded to the Insurance Department with the specific directions to conduct a hearing in compliance with the Administrative Agency Law, 2 Pa. C.S. §§ 101, 501-508, 701-704, and the Insurance Commissioner is directed to recuse herself and appoint an individual to assume her adjudicatory functions in the above-captioned matter. 3. Respondents' motion to quash for lack of standing is granted with respect to the reinsurers and is denied with respect to the policyholders; on remand, the fact finder is directed to make specific findings of fact regarding the status of each petitioner as a policyholder or reinsurer. 4. Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, policyholders and reinsurers, sought review of a decision of respondent Pennsylvania Insurance Department, which approved a plan of restructure and division filed by an insurer under the amendments to the General Association Act of 1988, 15 P.S. §§ 20101-20304.


OVERVIEW: An insurer proposed to divide and restructure in accordance with the General Association Act of 1988, 15 P.S. §§ 20101-20304, because of potential asbestos and environmental liabilities. Respondent Pennsylvania Insurance Department published notice of the plan, invited comments, and held informal hearings, but did not permit cross-examination or release expert reports. Respondent's commissioner approved the plan. Petitioners, policyholders and reinsurers, sought review. The court vacated and remanded. The court concluded that the General Assembly intended to interpose the formal procedures of the Administrative Agency Law (AAL), 2 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 101 et seq., between the determination after the informational hearing and judicial review, because the provision of 15 P.S. § 21207(d) for judicial review "in a manner prescribed by law" necessarily made the AAL applicable. The court found that because the commissioner could be considered to have prejudged the matter, due process precluded her from conducting the hearing and making a decision during the AAL phase of the proceedings. The court stated that petitioner policyholders had standing, but petitioner reinsurers did not.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order of respondent Pennsylvania Insurance Department approving an insurer's restructuring, because respondent had failed to conduct the formal procedure required by statute. The court remanded the matter for a formal hearing to be conducted in accordance with appropriate administrative procedures as to the issues raised by petitioner policyholders, but held that petitioner reinsurers had no standing.

Auberzinski v. Board of Probation & Parole


NO. 609 M.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


690 A.2d 776; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 88


November 18, 1996, Submitted on Briefs  
February 26, 1997, Decided  
February 26, 1997, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Counsel Amended February 28, 1997.



DISPOSITION: The department's preliminary objection in the nature of a demurrer is sustained.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate filed a petition for review in the nature of mandamus in the court's original jurisdiction, requesting that the court direct respondent Pennsylvania Board of Probation and Parole and respondent Pennsylvania Department of Corrections to release him on parole. Respondents both filed preliminary objections, including preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer.


OVERVIEW: While petitioner inmate was incarcerated, he applied for, and respondent Pennsylvania Department of Corrections (department) approved, several temporary home furloughs, which petitioner served without incident. The department granted petitioner another furlough, and then the department revoked it out of "caution" due to the "political climate" and "community sentiment." Respondent Pennsylvania Board of Probation and Parole (board) denied petitioner's application for parole. Petitioner claimed that the department's revocation of his furlough and the board's denial of parole violated his constitutional rights, and that the court should direct the department and the board to release him on parole. Both the department and the board filed preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer, which the court sustained. Petitioner's mandamus petition failed to allege sufficient facts to state a cause of action against the board. The facts as alleged by petitioner, that his temporary home furlough was approved and then arbitrarily revoked, did not alter the fact that petitioner had no constitutional right to participate in a pre-release program.


OUTCOME: The court sustained respondent Pennsylvania Board of Probation and Parole's preliminary objection in the nature of a demurrer because petitioner inmate's mandamus petition failed to allege sufficient facts to state a cause of action. The court sustained respondent Pennsylvania Department of Corrections' preliminary objection in the nature of a demurrer because one has no constitutional right to participate in a pre-release program.

Worley v. Pennsylvania Pub. Sch. Emples. Retirement Bd.


NO. 2009 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


689 A.2d 334; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 45


December 9, 1996, ARGUED  
January 31, 1997, Decided  
January 31, 1997, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied October 17, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Pa. LEXIS 2204.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 1994-19. State Agency PA Public School Employes' Retirement Board.



DISPOSITION: The board's order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner school employee sought review of a decision of respondent Pennsylvania Public School Employes' Retirement Board, which issued an order denying his request to purchase credit for nonschool service pursuant to 24 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8304.


OVERVIEW: After performing alternate civilian work as a conscientious objector, petitioner school employee received a certificate advising that he had completed the work in lieu of induction into the armed forces. When he joined the school employees' retirement program, he requested to purchase military retirement credit for the time spent in alternate service. Respondent, Pennsylvania Public School Employees' Retirement Board, denied his request, and petitioner sought review. The court affirmed respondent's order. The court stated that the record supported the conclusion that petitioner's service was not military service as that term was defined and utilized in 24 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 8102, 8304, and that because the statutory language was clear, its letter was not to be disregarded under the pretext of pursuing its spirit. The court stated that because such credit for military service was a benefit the commonwealth need not have offered, the legislature's restriction of the benefit to active military service was clearly not irrational and so did not violate U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court similarly found no violation of Pa. Const. art. I, §§ 3, 17, 26, or Pa. Const. art. III, § 16.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of respondent, Pennsylvania Public School Employees' Retirement Board, denying petitioner school employee's request to purchase credit for nonschool service. The court found that the alternate civilian work that petitioner performed as a conscientious objector did not qualify as the military service required for purchasing such credit.

 

515 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Weaver v. Pa. Bd. of Prob. & Parole


No. 496 M.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


688 A.2d 766; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 42


October 9, 1996, Submitted on Briefs  
January 30, 1997, Decided  
January 30, 1997, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent parole board filed preliminary objections with the court in response to a petition for writ of mandamus or other appropriate relief filed by petitioner inmate against respondents, parole board and department of corrections.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner inmate was convicted of and sentenced for rape and involuntary deviate sexual intercourse. He was repeatedly denied parole by respondent parole board for failing to participate in a treatment program for sex offenders. Petitioner sought review by seeking mandamus or other relief against respondents, parole board and department of corrections, and respondent parole board filed preliminary objections. The court dismissed respondent from the matter. The court stated that there was no protected liberty interest in receiving parole. Petitioner's placement of a constitutional patina on his challenge did not give the court jurisdiction to hear his appeal because at the core of any such appeal were the principles that a prisoner had a right to parole and could have challenged respondent parole board's exercise of its discretion. The court stated that mandamus did not lie because there was no constitutional prohibition against using petitioner's refusal to admit guilt as a basis for denying participation in the treatment program and a failure to complete that program successfully was a valid reason for denying parole.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed respondent parole board from petitioner inmate's action against respondents, parole board and department of corrections, for mandamus or other relief to review the failure to grant petitioner parole. The court found that there was no constitutionally protected liberty interest in receiving parole and the court therefore lacked jurisdiction to review, by mandamus or otherwise, respondent parole board's discretionary acts.

Olsen v. Pa. State Employes' Ret. Bd.


No. 1425 C.D. 1995, No. 2676 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


688 A.2d 255; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 32


September 9, 1996, Argued  
January 28, 1997, Decided  
January 28, 1997, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 1992-8. State Agency State Employes' Retirement Board.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Board is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner retiree sought review of two decisions of the Pennsylvania State Employees' Retirement Board, which determined that petitioner was ineligible to purchase credit for the three-year period when he was not an active employee of the commonwealth.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner retiree was employed by a state college when he left work in 1965 for three years to continue his studies under federal grants. He returned to state employment until he retired. Following a hearing granting petitioner's request to purchase credit for the three years of graduate study, respondent Pennsylvania State Employees' Retirement Board reversed the hearing examiner's decision, denying petitioner's request. Respondent concluded that the legislature did not intend to give 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5302(b)(1) retroactive effect. Section 5302(b)(1), relating to credited state service, provided that a member on leave without pay who was studying under a federal grant approved by the head of his department was be eligible for credit for such service. Under 4 Pa. Code § 243.4, credited state service was available to a member on leave without pay on March 1, 1974, or thereafter, who was studying under a federal grant approved by the head of his department. On appeal, the court affirmed respondent's decision, agreeing that § 5302(b)(1) did not provide for credit for service while on a leave of absence to study under a federal grant before March 1, 1974.


OUTCOME: The order of respondent Pennsylvania State Employees' Retirement Board was affirmed. The court agreed that the applicable statute did not provide for credit for service while on a leave of absence to study under a federal grant before March 1, 1974.

 

517 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Golden Triangle News v. Corbett


No. 1161 M.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


689 A.2d 974; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 65


December 20, 1996, HEARD  
January 23, 1997, Decided  
January 23, 1997, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Memorandum Opinion Redesignated Opinion and Ordered Published February 11, 1997.



DISPOSITION: Petitioners' request for a preliminary injunction is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a matter of original jurisdiction before the court, petitioners, adult bookstore operators, sought a preliminary injunction enjoining respondent state from enforcing an act governing the operation of adult bookstores. Petitioners' complaint alleged that the act violated their constitutional rights and alleged irreparable harm to them should the act be enforced.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, adult-bookstore operators, filed a petition seeking a declaration that 68 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5501-5509, (the act) was unconstitutional. Petitioners filed a motion seeking a preliminary injunction against respondent state from enforcing the act. The court denied the motion, holding that petitioners failed to demonstrate a probability of success on the merits, and did not demonstrate irreparable harm. The court found that the requirement that materials be displayed in well-lighted areas, which were open and visible from a common area, restricted only the manner in which materials could have been viewed, was content neutral. The court held that the restrictions were narrowly tailored to achieve the legitimate goals of reducing illicit sexual activity and the spread of communicable diseases. The court held that the provision abating violations of the act as nuisances was not a prior restraint, and held that the act was not unconstitutional vague. Finally the court held that the requirement that such businesses were to open to inspection at all reasonable times by inspectors, did not authorize a warrantless search in violation of Pa. Const. art. I, § 8.


OUTCOME: The court denied the request by petitioners, adult bookstore operators, for a preliminary injunction against respondent state from enforcing a statute governing the operation of adult bookstores. The court found that the challenged statute did not impose a prior restraint of free speech, did not chill the exercise of free expression rights, did not impermissibly restrict protected expression, and was not unconstitutionally vague.

Roxborough Manayunk Fed. S & L Ass'n v. Commonwealth


No. 82 F.R. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


687 A.2d 1202; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 4


December 12, 1996, Argued  
January 7, 1997, Decided  
January 7, 1997, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 91-3237. State Agency Board of Finance and Revenue.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed the order of the Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner savings and loan association sought review of a decision of the Board of Finance and Revenue (Pennsylvania), which issued an order upholding the resettlement by the Commonwealth of petitioner's mutual thrift institutions tax.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner savings and loan association failed to include in its net income, for purposes of its mutual thrift institutions tax obligation under 72 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 8501 - 8506, interest income earned from a demand deposit account with the Federal Home Loan Bank (FHLB). The Commonwealth resettled the tax by disallowing an exemption for the interest income from the FHLB demand deposit account, and the Board of Finance and Revenue upheld the resettlement. Petitioner sought review. The court affirmed the board's decision. The court stated that FHLB demand deposit accounts were not "obligations" of the United States, under 12 U.S.C.S. § 1433, that were exempted from taxation by the Commonwealth by federal statutes. The court also stated that petitioner's reliance on a departmental bulletin was misplaced, because the express language of the bulletin was that only notes and consolidated bonds of the FHLB were considered United States obligations, and because such lists were not intended to be exhaustive.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the board upholding the Commonwealth's resettlement of petitioner savings and loan association's mutual thrift institutions tax, because interest earned by petitioner on demand deposit accounts from a federal bank was not exempt from taxation as an obligation of the United States.

Puhlman v. Chester County Bd. of Assessment Appeals


No. 1577 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


687 A.2d 420; 1997 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 9


October 10, 1996, Argued  
January 6, 1997, Decided  
January 6, 1997, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied June 10, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Pa. LEXIS 1150.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 95-02582. Common Pleas Court of the County of Chester. Judge WOOD.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners, in their tax assessment appeal, sought review of the order of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Chester (Pennsylvania) which dismissed their equity complaint seeking to prohibit appellee county board of assessment appeals from applying its own class certification rules. The trial court held that the appellate procedures found in 72 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5020-518.1, 5350 provided an adequate remedy at law.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owners filed a class action appeal of tax assessments with appellee county board of assessment appeals. Appellee then promulgated procedures related to class certification and granted class certification to certain of appellants' groups and denied certification to others. Appellants filed a complaint in equity on appeal to the trial court, seeking to compel appellee to hear the appeals in the manner prescribed by statute without application of appellee's class action rules. The trial court dismissed, holding that appellants had an adequate remedy at law in the appellate procedures found in 72 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5020-518.1, 5350, and the only way for appellants to test appellee's certification procedures was to appeal a final order of appellee. Appellants sought reversal. The court affirmed, holding that appellee could hear class action tax assessment appeals under 72 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5349(c) of the Third Class County Assessment Law which provided appellants a statutorily prescribed administrative remedy and thus an adequate remedy at law; and appellants made only mere allegations of unconstitutionality, insufficient to confer equity jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order which dismissed appellant property owners' equity complaint challenging appellee county board of assessment appeals's application of its own class certification rules. The court held that the statutorily prescribed appellate procedures provided an adequate remedy at law to challenge the certification rules applied.

Commonwealth v. Snyder


No. 1426 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


688 A.2d 230; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 556


November 21, 1996, Submitted on Briefs  
December 31, 1996, Decided  
December 31, 1996, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 866-1995 C.D. Common Pleas Court of the County of Jefferson. Judge HENRY, President Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Jefferson County (Pennsylvania), that summarily convicted him of violating of the Punxsutawney, Pa. Ordinance No. 982, on the ground that the council exceeded its powers by enacting an ordinance which he believed declared junked vehicles to be a nuisance per se and abused its discretion when it sentenced him to 90 days in jail.


OVERVIEW: Appellant operated a bus company that provided charter bus service and transportation of students. He owned property where, for more than 15 years he stored numerous motor vehicles and parts to use for the repair of his vehicles. The borough council adopted Punxsutawney, Pa. Ordinance No. 982, which regulated the number of junk vehicles that could be stored on property. The borough charged appellant with committing a violation of the ordinance and ordered him to pay a fine. The trial court found appellant guilty, ordered him to pay the cost of prosecution, a fine, and sentenced him to 90 days in jail. Appellant argued that the trial court erred in finding him guilty of the ordinance and abused its discretion in sentencing him to jail. Appellant argued that the council exceeded powers by enacting an ordinance which he believed declared junked vehicles to be a nuisance per se. The appellate court reversed and held that the evidence was insufficient to prove the junk vehicles on appellant's property constituted a nuisance in fact. The court ruled that sentencing under the Crimes Code, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1105, did not control the penal provisions of violations of the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the conviction and held that the trial court erred as a matter of law in holding that the borough met its burden to prove that a nuisance in fact existed upon appellant's property. The court ruled that the trial court sentencing of appellant to 90 days in jail would have been erroneous, even if it had properly found him guilty of violating the ordinance.

Kaehly v. City of Pittsburgh


No. 60 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


687 A.2d 41; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 547


November 19, 1996, ARGUED  
December 24, 1996, Decided  
December 24, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied May 21, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. G.D. 95-20255. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Judge GALLO.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant vendors sought review of the judgment of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny (Pennsylvania), which denied their claim for preliminary injunction against appellees, city and stadium authority, which amended appellee city's vending ordinance, Pittsburgh, Pa., Code § 719.05, to prohibit vending within 30 feet of property owned by appellee stadium authority.


OVERVIEW: Appellant vendors sought a preliminary injunction after appellee city amended its vending ordinance, Pittsburgh, Pa., Code § 719.05, to prohibit appellants from operating within 30 feet of property owned by appellee stadium authority. Prior to the amendment, appellants could operate within 250 feet of the stadium wall on property belonging to appellee stadium authority. The trial court denied the request for injunction. Appellants sought review, claiming that they were deprived of their property and equal protection of the laws. The court affirmed, holding that appellants failed to demonstrate a clear legal right to injunctive relief. The court held that while the vending licenses were a protected property interest, appellants' licenses were not revoked, only the location in which they could operate was changed; and the amendment satisfied the rational basis test, because it was not arbitrary, beared a rational relationship to vending in appellee city, and appellants had no right to vend in a particular location, and thus appellants did not demonstrate a reasonable likelihood of success on the claim.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision which denied appellant vendors request for preliminary injunction against enforcement of appellee city's and stadium authority's vending ordinance, holding that appellants failed to demonstrate a clear legal right to relief. The court found that appellants had not been denied a property interest and that the ordinance beared a rational relationship to vending.

 

522 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Annenberg v. Commonwealth


No. 343 M.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


686 A.2d 1380; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 542


October 9, 1996, Argued  
December 23, 1996, Decided  
December 23, 1996, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [**1] The preliminary objections of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania and the Attorney General and the County of Montgomery et al. to the petition for review are sustained, and this case is transferred to the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County sitting in equity, with instructions to provide expedited consideration of the Petitioners' action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers filed a petition for review in the form of a declaratory judgment action with the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania against respondent state, its attorney general, the Board of Commissioners of the county, the Board of Assessment Appeals of the county, and the county. Petitioners claimed that the County Personal Property Tax Law, 72 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 4821-4902 was unconstitutional and respondents challenged jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner taxpayers owned stock of corporations that did not do business Pennsylvania and contended that the County Personal Property Tax Law, 72 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 4821-4902 was unconstitutional in taxing such stock. Petitioners challenged such taxation by filing a petition for review in the form of a declaratory judgment with the court. Respondent state, its attorney general, Board of Commissioners of the county, Board of Assessment Appeals of the county, and the county objected to the court's original jurisdiction. The court sustained respondents' objection and held that respondent Board of Assessment Appeals was not a proper forum because it had no expertise in deciding whether a statute violated the United States Constitution. As such, the court found that the original jurisdiction did properly lie in a court of equity. However, the court held that it did not have original jurisdiction of the matter because respondent state and its attorney general were not indispensable parties to the suit. The court held that respondent county was the taxing authority and that the proper forum for the original adjudication of the matter was the court of common pleas sitting in equity.


OUTCOME: The court sustained respondent state, attorney general, the Board of Commissioner of the county, the Board of Assessment Appeals of the county, and the county to petitioner taxpayers' petition for review. The court transferred the case to the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County sitting in equity, with instructions to provide expedited consideration of petitioners' action because the court did not have original jurisdiction.

Commonwealth v. Siemel


No. 110 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


686 A.2d 899; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 526


October 9, 1996, Submitted on Briefs  
December 17, 1996, Decided  
December 17, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied July 11, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Pa. LEXIS 1381.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 5823-93. Common Pleas Court of the County of Montgomery. Judge MOORE.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant lot owner sought review of the judgment of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Montgomery (Pennsylvania) which upheld appellant's summary conviction for violation of Lansdale, Pa., Code 876, prohibiting weed or grass growth on lots adjacent to private lots. Appellant argued that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague in its definition of terms.


OVERVIEW: Appellant lot owner owned a residential lot bordered on three sides by homes. Appellee borough notified appellant orally then in writing that she was in violation of Lansdale, Pa., Code 876, prohibiting excessive vegetation upon private premises adjacent to private premises, and requested that she cut the grass down to the six-inch height limit. After the grass reached 24 inches, appellee issued 3 citations for violation of the ordinance. Appellant was convicted in district court. The trial court upheld the summary conviction and fined appellant. Appellant sought review, challenging the ordinance as unconstitutionality vague in its definitions of "useful and ornamental" and "planted." The court affirmed, holding that the ordinance was enacted pursuant to the Borough Code, 53 P.S. § 46202, empowering boroughs to prohibit accumulation of garbage and rubbish on private ground; and the ordinance did not infringe upon appellant's due process rights as its terms could be defined according to their common meaning, the ordinance set a definite height to which vegetation could grow, and provided for notice by appellee before an attempt to abate the nuisance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment which affirmed appellant lot owner's summary conviction for violation of a borough's ordinance prohibiting weed growth on lots above a specified height. The court held that the ordinance's terms were not unconstitutionally vague and the ordinance provided for notice by appellee prior to abatement of the nuisance.

 

524 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Rood


No. 1437 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


686 A.2d 442; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 503


September 11, 1996, Argued  
December 9, 1996, Decided  
December 9, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied August 15, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Pa. LEXIS 1699.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 93-11,610. Common Pleas Court of the County of Lycoming. Judge KIESER.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hunter, convicted in the Common Pleas Court of the County of Lycoming (Pennsylvania) of violating three sections of the Game and Wildlife Code and fined, challenged his conviction, alleging that the evidence should have been suppressed because it was obtained by unconstitutional means.


OVERVIEW: Conservation officers investigated an anonymous tip that two untagged deer, had been killed illegally and were in a barn on the property where appellant hunter lived. On finding no one at the house, the officers looked around the property to locate someone who would know about the deer, and proceeded to the back of the house, climbing over a corral fence. They observed a barn and two hanging deer carcasses through the open barn door. The officers located appellant's stepson who stated that appellant had killed one deer, and informed the officers of appellant's location. The officers located appellant on the edge of a wooded area. Appellant admitted to the hunting, and signed a statement and consent form agreeing to allow the removal of the deer. Appellant was found guilty of violating three sections of the Game and Wildlife Code, 34 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 2307(a), 2321(a)(1), and 2711(a)(1). The court affirmed, holding that, while the initial search of the barn was unconstitutional and appellant's stepson's statement was inadmissible, appellant's statements and consent form, the after-acquired deer carcasses, and other evidence, were all properly admitted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that sufficient competent evidence supported conviction, and that appellant hunter's statements and consent form, the after-acquired deer carcasses, and other evidence, were all admissible, because the causal nexus between the original illegal search of the barn and the final search of the barn and removal of the deer was substantially attenuated by the independent voluntary actions on the part of appellant.

Smith v. Athens Twp. Auth.


No. 1974 C.D. 1995, No. 1975 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


685 A.2d 651; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 492


April 16, 1996, Argued  
November 27, 1996, Decided  
November 27, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petitions for Allowance of Appeal Denied May 6, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Pa. LEXIS 925.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 90MD000506 and 91CV000062. Common Pleas Court of the County of Bradford. Judge SMITH, President Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Court of Common Pleas of Bradford County dated March 30, 1995 at No. 91 CV 000062 and No. 90 MD 000506 is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, owners of trailer parks and apartment complex, challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Bradford County (Pennsylvania), which determined that an ordinance adopted by township supervisors, which established a lower tap-in fee for the municipal sewer system, was invalid and that a resolution of appellee township authority, which established a higher tap-in fee, controlled and correctly set the cost of sewer connection fees.


OVERVIEW: Appellee township authority was established under the Municipality Authorities Act (Act), 53 P.S. §§ 301-322. Appellants, owners of trailer parks and apartment complex, applied to appellee for tap-in permits. Appellants claimed that an ordinance enacted by township supervisors that established lower tap-in fees was applicable to them because it was in effect at the time they applied for their tap-in permits. Appellee argued and the trial court agreed that the ordinance was not valid and did not control tap-in fees because the supervisors did not have authority to set said fees. On appeal, the court affirmed the order of the trial court and held that under the Act, appellee had the exclusive power to regulate sewer rates and tap-in fees. As such appellee's resolution establishing the higher tap-in fees was applicable and found to be reasonable and uniform. The court also concluded that the trial court had not abused its discretion in denying appellants' motion to amend the complaint for the third time after the close of testimony to raise constitutional issues.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court, which found that the lower rates imposed by an ordinance that was enacted by township supervisors was invalid because pursuant to the Municipality Authorities Act, appellee township authority had the exclusive power to set all rates and charges, including tap-in fees necessary for sewer systems.

Juliano v. Workmen's Compensation Appeal Bd. (Custodis-Cottrell)


No. 2392 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


683 A.2d 1319; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 433


August 16, 1996, Submitted On Briefs  
October 22, 1996, Decided  
October 22, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied March 20, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Pa. LEXIS 572.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. A94-1867. State Agency Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant employee petitioned for review of an order of respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board (Pennsylvania), which applied the Workers' Compensation Act, 77 P.S. § 511(2) (1993), to suspend his benefits during his incarceration.


OVERVIEW: Claimant employee began receiving workers' compensation benefits for a compensable injury to his right knee. The employer petitioned for suspension of benefits, and alleged that claimant was incarcerated. The workers' compensation judge ordered the employer to pay one-half of the benefits to claimant's family and one-half to the department of corrections to defray the costs for claimant's food, clothing, and shelter. Respondent board reversed and applied the Workers' Compensation Act, 77 P.S. § 511(2) (1993), which was enacted after the injury and incarceration. Respondent suspended claimant's benefits during the period of incarceration after his conviction. Claimant petitioned for review. The court affirmed because § 511(2) had no retroactive effect on claimant's substantive right to compensation following his release from incarceration. The court held that, regardless of the incarceration date, respondent could suspend benefits during periods of incarceration after the August 31, 1993, effective date of § 511(2).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed because respondent board's suspension of benefits did not amount to an illegal retroactive cessation of claimant's substantive right to workers' compensation benefits after his release from incarceration.

The court further held that there was substantial evidence supporting the board's factual finding.

Medic-9 Paramedic Serv. v. Dep't of Health


No. 2743 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


683 A.2d 1275; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 418


September 13, 1996, Argued  
October 17, 1996, Decided  
October 17, 1996, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. EMS-002-93. State Agency Department of Health.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner emergency medical service sought review of the decision of respondent Pennsylvania Department of Health, which concluded that the proper criteria for the licensing of advanced life support ambulance services were set forth in the Emergency Medical Services Act (the Act), 35 P.S. § 6932(h) (1985), and that there was no language within the Act to require that as a precondition to licensure, there was a need for additional services.


OVERVIEW: A competing ambulance service was approved to provide advanced life-support ambulance services to a particular geographic region that was already serviced by petitioner emergency medical service. Respondent department of health determined that the Emergency Medical Services Act (the Act), 35 P.S. § 6932(h) (1985), contained the correct criteria for the licensure of advanced life support (ALS) ambulance services and that the Act did not require, as a precondition to receiving a license, that the competing ambulance service demonstrate that the services were needed. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision of respondent. The court found that the Act was unambiguous, and as such § 6932(h) clearly set forth the correct standard for the licensure of ALS ambulance services. The court also found that nowhere in the Act did it require a prospective licensee to demonstrate, as a precondition to licensure, that there was a need for the ALS ambulance services.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of respondent department of health to grant a license to petitioner emergency medical service because it had met the requirements of the Emergency Medical Services Act (Act), which plainly set forth the criteria to be used when licensing an advanced life support (ALS) ambulance service and nowhere in the Act was "need" for the services provided as a criterion for licensure of ALS ambulance services.

Braig v. Pennsylvania State Employes' Retirement Bd.


No. 209 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


682 A.2d 881; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 381


June 13, 1996, Argued  
September 12, 1996, Decided  
September 12, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Discontinued November 8, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 1992-19, 1992-20, and 1992-21. State Agency State Employes' Retirement Board.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner claimants sought review of an order of respondent State Agency State Employees' Retirement Board (Pennsylvania), which denied claimant's request for interest benefits withheld by the state retirement system pursuant to a purported pension forfeiture.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner claimants sought review of an order of respondent state retirement board, which denied claimant's request for interest benefits withheld by the state retirement system pursuant to a purported pension forfeiture. Petitioners were all judges or represented the interest of judges who had been removed from office pursuant to Pa. Const. art. V, § 18(1) and were subject to Pa. Const. art. V, § 16(b) determined to be a pension forfeiture provision. The court held that because petitioner's retirement and pension benefits constituted both liquidated and enforceable debts, respondent's failure to pay those debts when they became due was characterized as a wrongful withholding of petitioner's benefits. The court held that neither the history nor the language of the Pennsylvania Constitution supported a conclusion that Pa. Const. art. V, § 16(b) was meant to create a broad pension forfeiture provision. Accordingly, the court found that petitioner's petition for interest presented a cognizable claim under the common law, which imposed a duty upon respondent to pay interest as damages for delay in discharging its debt.


OUTCOME: The court held that petitioner claimants were entitled to an award of interest on retirement benefits due to petitioners from respondent state retirement board and, therefore, reversed respondent's order that denied petitioner's request for interest on retirement and remanded the case to respondent. The court held that petitioner's benefits were liquidated and enforceable debts and, therefore, payable by respondent.

League of Women Voters v. Commonwealth


No. 711 M.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


683 A.2d 685; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 408


September 9, 1996, HEARD  
September 12, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Memorandum Opinion Redesignated Opinion and Ordered Published October 1, 1996.



DISPOSITION: Petitioners' request for preliminary injunctive relief denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner political action groups sought a preliminary injunction to restrain respondents, commonwealth and related officials, from enforcing provisions of the General Appropriation Act. Petitioners alleged it violated Pa. Const. art. II, § 1 and Pa. Const. art. III, §§ 2, 4, 5 and the Sunshine Act, 65 P.S. § 271 et seq., by virtue of a conference committee's changes to the different versions of the bill passed by the legislature.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner political action groups sought review in the nature of a complaint in equity in the court's original jurisdiction, and alleged that the general assembly, in its enactment of the General Appropriation Act (GAA) violated Pa. Const. art. II, § 1 and Pa. Const. art. III, §§ 2, 4, 5 and the Sunshine Act, 65 P.S. § 271 et seq., when a conference committee compiled an extensive report that contained numerous changes from one or both versions of the bill adopted by the two chambers of the legislature. Petitioners requested the court to declare the GAA, or those parts inserted by committee, unconstitutional and to enjoin respondents, commonwealth and related officials, from enforcing those parts. Denying petitioners' request, the court held that the GAA was constitutionally enacted where the legislature followed proper procedures, and the committee's report did not violate the Sunshine Act where it was introduced at the open meeting at which the committee took official action.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner political action groups' petition for preliminary injunction. The General Appropriation Act was constitutionally enacted where the legislature followed proper procedures, and the committee report did not violate the Sunshine Act because it was introduced at a subsequent open meeting.

Ligonier Tavern v. Workmen's Compensation Appeal Bd. (Walker)

COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. A94-0440. State Agency Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board.



DISPOSITION: Order of the Board is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employer sought review of an order of respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board, which affirmed a decision of a hearing referee awarding "additional compensation" benefits to a minor claimant who suffered a workplace injury under § 320 of the Workers' Compensation Act, 77 P.S. § 672, because of petitioner's violation of the Child Labor Law, 43 P.S. §§ 41-71, in employing the claimant.


OVERVIEW: A 17-year-old claimant was injured while working for petitioner employer, and a workers' compensation hearing referee awarded claimant compensation for the injury, as well as 50 percent additional compensation due to petitioner's hiring of claimant without a work permit in violation of the Child Labor Law, 43 P.S. §§ 41-71. Respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board affirmed the referee's decision, and petitioner sought review. The court affirmed respondent's order and directed petitioner to pay claimant all past due compensation including the additional compensation as ordered by the referee. The court found that respondent did not err in affirming the referee's decision. The court held that § 320 of the Workers' Compensation Act, 77 P.S. § 672, was clear and unambiguous, and the law's requirement of 50 percent additional compensation was reasonable in amount. The court found that the law's classification of minors under age 18 was established with good reason, was uniform and not special legislation, and therefore did not deny equal protection of the laws under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1, and Pa. Const. art. III, § 18.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board's order and directed petitioner employer to pay claimant all past due compensation and additional compensation as ordered by the referee. The court held that the law requiring payment of 50 percent additional compensation for the violation of a child labor law was clear and unambiguous, the award was reasonable in amount, and did not deny petitioner equal protection of the laws.

Gueson v. Reed


No. 593 M.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


679 A.2d 284; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 304


June 27, 1996, Heard  
July 11, 1996, Decided  
July 11, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Memorandum Opinion Redesignated Opinion and Ordered Published July 19, 1996.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner health care providers requested preliminary injunctions against respondents, malpractice insurance fund and state boards of medicine, osteopathic medicine and podiatry, seeking to prohibit the respondent boards from suspending petitioners' licenses because of nonpayment of emergency surcharges for medical malpractice insurance in excess of basic coverage under 40 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1301.701.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner health care providers requested preliminary injunctions against respondents, malpractice insurance fund and state boards of medicine, osteopathic medicine, and podiatry, who threatened suspension of petitioners' licenses because of nonpayment of emergency surcharges for medical malpractice insurance. The court denied the request, holding that petitioners did not meet the criteria for a preliminary injunction. The court noted that some petitioners showed immediate and irreparable harm, but others refused to pay on principle, and self-inflicted harm did not qualify as irreparable. The court held that balancing petitioners' interest with the public interest, greater harm would result to malpractice victims if the relief was granted. The court held that the injunction could not restore the status quo, as not all petitioners held the same status. The court held that petitioners did not establish that the administrative remedies were inadequate, but that the more appropriate relief was administrative hearings for each petitioner to present its own case and for respondent boards to interpret the Health Care Services Malpractice Act, 40 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1301.701(f).


OUTCOME: The court denied the request by petitioner health care providers for preliminary injunctions against respondents, malpractice insurance fund and state boards of medicine, osteopathic medicine, and podiatry, because although petitioners established immediate and irreparable harm, they did not have all the same status, and they did not establish that the administrative remedies were inadequate.

COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant non-residents sought review of an order by the Common Pleas Court of Northampton (Pennsylvania), which upheld as valid and enforceable appellee city's one-year pre-employment residency requirement for firefighters, pursuant to 53 P.S. § 39864.


OVERVIEW: Appellee city adopted a resolution that did not require residency prior to applying for a firefighter position. Appellee residents commenced an equity action to prevent the city from considering non-residents. The trial court judge held that the Civil Service Act's pre-employment residency requirement prevented city from considering non-residents. The city did not appeal. Appellant non-residents petitioned to the trial court for intervention, which was granted. Appellants contended that the one-year pre-employment residency requirement was not related to civil service but affected matters relating merely to personnel and administration of the city and were exclusively within the city's province and were not a matter of statewide concern. The court held that the residency requirement was a statewide concern. Appellants contended that the requirement impaired their right to travel under U.S. Const. art. IV, § 2. The court held that the residency requirement must be analyzed under the rational relationship test. The court also noted that there were no cases which held that an individual had a fundamental right to public employment. The order of the court of common pleas was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of common pleas, which held that appellee city was required to follow the one-year pre-employment residency requirement for firefighter applicants, was affirmed. The court held that the residency requirement was a statewide concern.

State Troopers Ass'n v. State Employes' Retirement Bd.


No. 2022 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


677 A.2d 1329; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 257


April 17, 1996, Argued  
June 20, 1996, Decided  
June 20, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied February 13, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Pa. LEXIS 399.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 1991-13 (A - X). State Agency State Employes' Retirement Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, police officers and union, challenged the order of the State Employees' Retirement Board, which dismissed petitioners' objections to the implementation of an arbitration award between the State of Pennsylvania and the Conference of Pennsylvania State Police Lodges. The award changed benefit provisions of the State Employees' Retirement Code, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5101-5956.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners were retired police officers who were enrolled in the State Employees' Retirement System and the union that represented the officers in collective bargaining. Petitioner union entered into a collective bargaining agreement with the state that affected petitioner police officer's retirement benefits and the retirement system adjusted petitioner police officer's pension accordingly. Petitioner police officer's challenged the adjustment and the retirement system resumed paying the unadjusted amounts, subject to adjustment at the conclusion of all appeals. The court, on appeal, affirmed appellee State Employees' Retirement Board's decision to adjust petitioner police officer's pension benefits and found that petitioner police officers belonged to a collective bargaining unit that was authorized to enter into binding arbitration on petitioner police officers' behalf, under Act 111, 43 P.S. §§ 217.1-.10. The court found that the State Employees' Retirement Code, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5955, did not prohibit petitioner police officers' benefits from being affected by arbitration awards, but only prohibited collective bargaining from affecting pension rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court dismissing petitioners', police officers and union, objections to implementation of an arbitration award because the Retirement Code did not prohibit collective bargaining over pension benefits nor did it prohibit pension benefits from being affected by arbitration awards, but only prohibited collective bargaining agreements from determining pension rights.

Shohola Falls Trails End Prop. Owners Ass'n v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


NO. 2189 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


679 A.2d 1335; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 255


April 18, 1996, ARGUED  
June 20, 1996, Decided  
June 20, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied June 13, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Pa. LEXIS 1198.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 342 - 1994 - Civil. Common Pleas Court of the County of Pike. Judge THOMSON, JR., President Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the trial court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant camp site owners challenged the judgment of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Pike (Pennsylvania), which affirmed appellee Zoning Hearing Board of Shohola Township's denial of appellant's application for a zoning permit and request for a variance under Shohola, Pa., Zoning Ordinance 44, §528.


OVERVIEW: Appellant campsite owners were the owners and homeowners association for a tract of land used for camping and campsites. Appellee Zoning Hearing Board of Shohola Township, required appellants to put density limits in their deed restrictions, which appellants failed to enforce. Appellee subsequently passed a zoning ordinance in order to impose the density limits on the campsites, Shohola, Pa., Zoning Ordinance 44, § 528. Appellants unsuccessfully challenged the validity of the zoning ordinance, and requested a variance from the ordinance. The court, on appeal, affirmed the trial court's finding that appellants had not established that they were entitled to a variance, and held that appellee was not engaged in spot zoning. The court found that appellants were not being singled out and treated differently from similar land, and that the density and setback requirements applied to motor homes and recreational vehicles. The court found that the ordinance did not deprive appellants of the use of their land as a camping area, that its intended use was temporary camping and not permanent residency, and that the density and setback requirements were valid zoning restrictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision which rejected appellant campsite owners' appeal for a zoning variance, because the zoning ordinance was created to enforce restrictive covenants on the properties, was related to density and setbacks, and appellants failed to show that appellee Zoning Hearing Board of Shohola Township engaged in illegal spot zoning or that they were entitled to a variance.

PPG Indus. v. Commonwealth


NO. 2355 C.D. 1987


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


681 A.2d 832; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 259


May 15, 1996, ARGUED  
June 19, 1996, Decided  
June 19, 1996, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. R-10,904. State Agency Board of Finance & Revenue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporation field exceptions, pursuant to Pa. R .App. P. 1571(i), to a panel decision of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, which affirmed the order of respondent finance review board, resettling petitioner's capital stock tax for the year 1983 by applying the manufacturing exemption for capital stock to only that portion of petitioner's corporate headquarters which was related to manufacturing within the state.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, a Pennsylvania corporation, filed exceptions to the order of the court, which affirmed the order of respondent finance review board, resettling its capital stock tax by applying the manufacturing exemption for capital stock to only that portion of petitioner's corporate headquarters which was related to manufacturing within the state. The court held that respondent's method of tax treatment did not discriminate, because after a corporation elected the method of computation most beneficial to it, all corporations, foreign and domestic, were treated alike. Furthermore, the court held that respondent properly applied 72 P.S. § 7602(a), and was not required to apply § 7602(a). Additionally, the court also ruled that respondent's application of the manufacturing exemption did not violate either the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3, the Equal Protection Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1, or the Pennsylvania Uniformity Clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed petitioner corporation's exceptions to the trial court's panel decision, and reaffirmed its order affirming the order of respondent board, after petitioner sought review of respondent's order resettling its capital stock tax. The court affirmed respondent's order and entered judgment in the commonwealth's favor. The court held that, under respondent's nondiscriminatory tax treatment, all corporations are treated alike.

 

539 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Airpark Int'l I v. Interboro Sch. Dist.


NO. 1329 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


677 A.2d 388; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 300


February 5, 1996, ARGUED  
June 7, 1996, Decided  
June 7, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted June 23, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Pa. LEXIS 1200.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 94-07010. State Agency, Common Pleas Court of the county of Delaware. Judge LABRUM, JR.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania) sustaining appellee commercial parking lot companies' statutory appeal and invalidating appellant's tax on commercial parking lots.


OVERVIEW: Appellee commercial parking operators within appellant school district successfully challenged a tax imposed on commercial parking lots that served an airport. The tax was for the general public use of funding education. The trial court held the tax was an invalid business privilege tax or mercantile tax on gross receipts in violation of the Reform Act, 72 P.S. § 4750.533 (1988). On appeal, the court found the parking lot tax was neither a mercantile tax nor a business privilege tax which violated the Reform Act, but was a tax upon the transaction fees for parking in commercial parking lots. The measure of the tax is simply six percent of "the fees paid." While appellees paid pay more taxes than others within the school district, it was the result of a reasonable classification of taxpayers and was not to benefit one group at the expense of another. The court held appellees failed to prove the parking lot tax was excessive or unreasonable or that the resolution is unlawful, under the Local Tax Enabling Act, 53 P.S. § 6906 (1965). The tax was valid.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed because, even strictly construed, appellant school district tax on commercial parking lots was a valid, transaction tax permitted by the Local Tax Enabling Act and it was not a business privilege tax on gross receipts or a mercantile tax on gross receipts prohibited by the Reform Act.

Commonwealth v. Mercadante


No. 100 C.D. 1996


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


676 A.2d 1309; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 218


April 19, 1996, ARGUED  
May 24, 1996, Decided  
May 24, 1996, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEALED From No. 1290 S.D. 1994. Common Pleas Court of the county of Fayette. Judge WAGNER.



DISPOSITION: The trial court's order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commonwealth challenged the order of the Fayette County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania), granting appellee restaurant owner's motion in arrest of judgment. Appellant contended that an ordinance taxing gross receipts of vendors of good but not services, was reasonable and legitimate, and that the ordinance did not violate the equal protection clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1, or the uniformity clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1.


OVERVIEW: Appellee restaurant owner was prosecuted and found guilty of violating Uniontown, Pa., Ordinance 1124, § 4, (1968), by failing to file gross receipt tax returns. The trial court subsequently arrested judgment, concluding that the ordinance was unconstitutional. Specifically, the trial court held that, because the ordinance taxed businesses selling goods, as well as restaurants and amusements, and not businesses providing services, it violated the equal protection clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1, or the uniformity clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1. Appellant commonwealth sought review, and the court affirmed the trial court. The court held that appellee had met his heavy burden, and demonstrated that the classification, made for purposes of taxation, was unreasonable. Furthermore, the court found that, because there was no legitimate distinction between the classes of vendors, the ordinance unconstitutionally imposed substantially unequal tax burdens, and was therefore clearly, palpably, and plainly unconstitutional. Based on the record, the court noted that the goods providers and the service providers utilized city services to similar extents and were similarly situated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order granting appellee restaurant owner's motion in arrest of judgment, in a dispute where appellant commonwealth claimed that a municipal ordinance that taxed gross receipts of vendors of good but not services, was reasonable, legitimate, and constitutional. The court held that there was no legitimate distinction between vendors, and the ordinance unconstitutionally imposed substantially unequal tax burdens.

Cohen v. State Bd. of Med.


No. 1024 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


676 A.2d 1277; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 212


April 15, 1996, Argued  
May 22, 1996, Decided  
May 22, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied November 6, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Pa. LEXIS 2176.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 0028-49-95. State Agency State Board of Medicine.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner physician appealed orders of respondent State Board of Medicine (Pennsylvania), which suspended and revoked petitioner's license to practice medicine under 63 P.S. § 422.40 (a). Appellant contended that substantial evidence did not support the revocation of his medical license, alleged prosecutorial misconduct, claimed that prosecution was barred by operation of laches, and that his due process rights were violated.


OVERVIEW: Respondent State Board of Medicine issued an order suspending petitioner physician's license to practice medicine under 63 P.S. § 422.40 (a). Following a formal hearing, the hearing examiner issued an order that sustained the suspension of the license to practice medicine for five years. Without taking additional evidence, respondent adopted many of the findings of the hearing examiner as its own, made additional findings from the evidence of record, and revoked petitioner's medical license. Petitioner sought review. The court affirmed both adjudications and orders of respondent for suspension and revocation. The court held that testimony and medical charts provided substantial evidence of petitioner's prescribing habits, prosecutorial conduct, a criminal concept, was inapplicable in petitioner's civil case, laches had not attached, because the record showed no lack of due diligence or unjustified delay, and petitioner's due process rights were not violated by witness credibility not being considered at the preliminary hearing, because witness credibility was not an issue at that stage, and respondent was the ultimate fact finder.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed two orders of respondent State Board of Medicine, which resulted in the suspension and ultimate revocation of petitioner physician's license to practice medicine. The court held that substantial testimony and documentary evidence supported the license revocation, prosecutorial misconduct was inapplicable to petitioner's civil case, laches did not attach, and petitioner's due process rights were not violated.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district sought review of the decision from the Common Pleas Court of the County of Dauphin (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a decree nisi that the tax imposed by appellant upon the privilege of leasing tax exempt realty was invalid.


OVERVIEW: Appellant school district sought review of the trial court's ruling that tax imposed by appellant on tax exempt realty was invalid. Appellees, city and other taxpayers filed a petition for review and requested for supersedeas notice from imposition of tax action for declaratory judgment. The trial court concluded that the tax imposed by appellant was invalid. Appellant filed a motion for post-trial. On appeal, the court dismissed appellants' appeal. The court held that the trial court erred in concluding that the tax on the privilege of leasing tax-exempt realty imposed by appellant was invalid. The court held that the subject tax herein did not violate the Uniformity Clause of the Pennsylvania Constitution or the Equal Protection Clause of the United States Constitution. The court reversed the decision of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decree nisi in favor of appellees, city and other taxpayers, because the tax on the privilege of leasing tax-exempt realty imposed by appellant was invalid.

Ruszin v. Dep't of Labor & Indus. Bureau of Workers Comp.


NO. 351 M.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


675 A.2d 366; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 165


March 13, 1996, ARGUED  
April 22, 1996, Decided  
April 22, 1996, FILED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employees and respondent employers both sought summary judgment in this declaratory judgment action to determine whether petitioners had to pursue their administrative remedies before they could maintain this action and, if not, were there questions of material fact precluding the grant of summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employers claimed hearing losses and sought to have the provision that reduced the time period to pursue hearing loss claims declared unconstitutional, and both petitioners and respondent employers sought summary judgment in this declaratory judgment action to determine whether petitioners had to pursue their administrative remedies before they could maintain this action and, if not, were there questions of material fact precluding the grant of summary judgment. The court held that petitioners could maintain this action, granted summary judgment against one petitioner as he had no complete hearing loss, but because the question of whether the other petitioners were aggrieved was a material fact, their motion for summary judgment was denied. If this were merely a matter of petitioners seeking benefits, this would have been a matter over which a workers' compensation tribunal had exclusive jurisdiction. However, petitioners sought to have the provision that reduced the time period to pursue hearing loss claims declared unconstitutional. A workers' compensation tribunal had no jurisdiction to determine the constitutional validity of its own enabling legislation.


OUTCOME: The court held that petitioner employees could maintain this declaratory judgment action, granted summary judgment against one petitioner but because the question of whether the other petitioners were aggrieved was a material fact, their motion for summary judgment was denied. The court held that the workers' compensation tribunal had no jurisdiction to determine the constitutional validity of its own enabling legislation.

Graff v. Scanlan


No. 3205 C.D. 1994


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


673 A.2d 1028; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 110


October 16, 1995, Argued  
March 22, 1996, Decided  
March 22, 1996, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEALED From No. 91-11497. Common Pleas Court of the county of Chester. Judge SHENKIN.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant adjacent landowners sought review of a decision from the Common Pleas Court of Chester County (Pennsylvania), that confirmed a report of a Board of View finding that a private road across portions of appellants property should be opened. Appellants asserted that appellee property owners had an existing easement over another lot and therefore, appellees' request was not a strict necessity.


OVERVIEW: Appellant adjacent landowners sought review of the decision that appellee property owners' lot was not landlocked because of an easement giving access across another lot in the subdivision. Appellees' had originally owned appellants lots and upon selling the lots, appellees failed to reserve an easement across the property to gain access to a public drive. Appellees filed for equitable relief in the trial court which denied appellees request, finding appellees' had neither an express or implied easement over appellants' property. Appellants filed a second suit requesting a board of view to determine the necessity of a private roadway, pursuant to the Private Road Act, 36 P.S. § 2731, from appellees property through appellants property to the public drive. The board of view found a necessity and ordered the roadway to be opened. Appellants sought review of the decision and the trial court affirmed. Appellants asserted that appellees' land was not landlocked and appellees did not qualify for an easement by necessity. The court reversed, finding that because appellees had created the situation of their landlocked status, that could not be the basis of a finding of strict necessity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court affirming the board of views' order to open a roadway from appellees property owners' property across appellant adjoining landowners' property to a public drive. The court held that because appellees had created this situation, appellees could not claim strict necessity and any inconvenience caused appellees in using an existing easement over another lot were not factors.

Belle Vernon Area Sch. Dist. v. Teamsters Local Union No. 782
