DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district sought review of an order from the Common Pleas Court (Pennsylvania), which denied appellant's petition to vacate a labor arbitration award upholding appellee bus driver's grievance against appellant for violating a collective bargaining agreement.


OVERVIEW: When a job was awarded to another employee, appellee bus driver filed a grievance against appellant school district under a collective bargaining agreement between appellee union and appellant. A trial court denied appellant's petition to vacate an arbitration award, which upheld appellee bus driver's grievance and ordered appellant to award the job to appellee bus driver. Appellant sought review. The court affirmed the trial court's order. The court determined that appellant's action in posting a mechanic position for bids evidenced that appellant believed that the bidding procedure under the collective bargaining agreement also applied to any vacancies occurring in the positions covered under the agreement. The court found that a move from bus driver to mechanic was considered an advancement and that the arbitrator's determination that appellant was required to fill the vacancy based upon a seniority provision derived its essence from the agreement. The court determined that the arbitrator properly concluded that the Veterans Preference Act, 51 Pa. Const. Stat. § 7104(a), was not applicable and that the mechanic job should have been awarded to appellee bus driver.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a trial court's order denying appellant school district's petition to vacate a labor arbitration award upholding appellee bus driver's grievance against appellant for violating a collective bargaining agreement. The court found that a move from bus driver to mechanic was considered an advancement and that the arbitrator's determination that the vacancy had to be filled based on seniority derived its essence from the agreement.

Lykins v. Workmen's Comp. Appeal Bd. (new Castle Foundry)


No. 1288 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


671 A.2d 253; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 27


November 14, 1995, Argued  
January 25, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted September 24, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1878.



DISPOSITION: The Board's order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee appealed an order of respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board (Pennsylvania), which reversed an order of the Workers' Compensation Judge (WCJ) denying the petition for modification of respondent employer. Respondent board stated that the date on which a claimant qualified to receive unemployment compensation was the critical date for determining whether the amended 77 P.S. § 71(a) was applicable in a given case.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employee sustained an injury while in the course of his employment with respondent employer, and collected total disability benefits. Subsequently, he also began to receive unemployment compensation benefits. Respondent filed a modification petition seeking a credit against petitioner's total disability benefits, and the Workers' Compensation Judge denied the petition. The Worker's Compensation Appeals Board (board) reversed the decision, and the court affirmed the board's judgment, holding that petitioner had a substantive right to benefits as provided by the statute in force at the time he qualified. In order for petitioner to have had a right to collect workers' compensation and unemployment compensation under the provisions of the pre-amendment version of 77 P.S. § 71(a), that right had to be fixed before the effective date of the amended statute. The court concluded that because petitioner did not qualify until after the amendment went into effect, he never established a substantive right to collect both workers' compensation and unemployment compensation benefits.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board and held that petitioner employee had a substantive right to workers' compensation and unemployment compensation benefits as provided by the statute in force at the time petitioner qualified for compensation.

 

550 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Maute v. Frank


NO. 227 M.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


670 A.2d 737; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 37


December 1, 1995, Submitted On Briefs  
January 22, 1996, Decided  
January 22, 1996, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [**1] The Prison Officials' preliminary objections are sustained and Maute's petition for review is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prisoner, filed an action in mandamus seeking relief from appellee prison officials' alleged violations of religious freedom.


OVERVIEW: The court dismissed appellant prisoner's action in mandamus and sustained the preliminary objections of appellee prison officials because it found that appellant's right to practice his Native American religion did not include the right to have the certain religious items. The court found that appellant failed to show that he had a clear right to relief and that the prison officials had a corresponding legal duty under law to provide relief. The court found that the prison officials utilized a balancing test to determine whether the appellant's requested religious articles were consistent with the orderly administration of the prison. The court determined that appellant had a right to practice his religion, but that Pa. Const. art. 1, § 3 and the Religious Freedom Restoration Act, 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 2000bb-2000bb-4 did not automatically require that prisoners be allowed the religious items they claimed were necessary to practice the religion. The court therefore dismissed appellant's mandamus action because it involved a discretionary decision on the part of the prison officials and an adequate remedy at law existed since appellant could seek relief under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed appellant prisoner's action in mandamus because appellee prison officials' decision to restrict appellant's access to religious items was a discretionary decision and consistent with the orderly administration of the prison.

P.J.S. v. Pennsylvania State Ethics Comm'n


No. 230 M.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


669 A.2d 1105; 1996 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 11


August 22, 1995, Argued  
January 8, 1996, Decided  
January 8, 1996, FILED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent state ethics commission filed preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer to petitioner attorney's request for review seeking declaratory judgment to resolve the question of whether respondent had jurisdiction to regulate his conduct as a city solicitor and also seeking a permanent injunction.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney served as a city solicitor. Respondent state ethics commission investigated a complaint that petitioner violated the State Ethics Act. Petitioner sought declaratory judgment, claiming respondent did not have jurisdiction, and a permanent injunction to enjoin respondent's proceedings. Respondent filed preliminary objections and claimed declaratory judgment was unavailable to petitioner because its investigation was within the exclusive jurisdiction of a tribunal other than the court. The court held that declaratory judgment was not precluded on this basis because petitioner's claim raised the issue of respondent's jurisdiction and whether its exercise of authority was constitutional. Additionally, the court held that declaratory judgment was available because petitioner properly invoked the proceeding to determine whether, under existing law, he was subject to respondent's jurisdiction. Because there were factual disputes concerning whether petitioner was a public employee and whether a permanent injunction was necessary to avoid injury for which damages would not provide adequate compensation, the court overruled respondent's preliminary objections.


OUTCOME: The court overruled respondent state ethics commission's preliminary objections in the nature of demurrer to petitioner attorney's request for review because declaratory judgment was not precluded on petitioner's claims and there were factual disputes which could not be determined on preliminary objections.

Commonwealth v. City of Cumberland


No. 2879 C.D. 1994


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


668 A.2d 275; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 608


September 15, 1995, Argued  
December 7, 1995, Decided  
December 7, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied September 5, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1709.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. Criminal Action Nos. 327, 328 and 329. Common Pleas Court of the county of Bedford. Judge HOWSARE, President Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged an order from the Common Pleas Court of Bedford County (Pennsylvania), which granted, in part a writ of habeas corpus to appellees, municipality, water company, and superintendent, and dismissed appellant's criminal complaint against them.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state filed a suit against appellees, municipality, water company, and superintendent, for violating the Clean Streams Act (the "Act"), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 35, § 691.1 et seq., and the Fish and Boat Code, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 30, § 101 et seq., and appellees filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus to dismiss the complaint because the charges were brought under penalty provisions and not substantive provisions. The lower court agreed and granted appellees' writ of habeas corpus only with respect to the charges under the act. On appeal, the court reversed the order. The court held that appellant did charge appellees under a penalty provision of the act because the crime was clearly defined as proscribed conduct in another section of the act and referred to appellant's provision as the penalty provision. The court also held that the lower court erred in dismissing the charges because although there was a defect in appellant's complaint, appellees were informed of the conduct that appellant deemed actionable, and, as such, actual prejudice to appellees' rights did not occur to warrant dismissal.


OUTCOME: Judgment in favor of dismissing appellant state's complaint against appellees, municipality, water company, and superintendent, was reversed on the grounds that appellant did erroneously charge appellees under statutory penalty provisions but the defect in appellant's complaint sufficed to inform appellees of the actionable conduct and, as such, did not prejudice appellees' rights.

In re Condemnation by the Pa. Turnpike Comm'n


No. 81 C.D. 1995, No. 82 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


668 A.2d 231; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 533


June 8, 1995, Argued  
November 30, 1995, Decided  
November 30, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied April 30, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Pa. LEXIS 869.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 903 and 905 of 1990. Common Pleas Court of the county of Westmoreland. Judge SCHERER.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant condemnees sought review of the decision of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Westmoreland (Pennsylvania), which denied appellants' petition to open and set aside a satisfaction and discontinuance in favor of appellant condemnor.


OVERVIEW: Appellee condemnor filed a Declaration of Taking of two parcels of property owned by appellant condemnees. The parties agreed to a settlement regarding the taking and signed a settlement agreement. Appellants filed a petition to open and set aside the satisfaction and discontinuance, believing that appellee failed to pay them delay damages under the Eminent Domain Code, 26 Pa. Stat. § 611. The trial court, however, denied the petition, holding that the agreement represented the entire agreement of the parties and included all amounts payable. The court reversed, holding held that appellants had a legal right to delay damages calculated at the time of payment for the period during which payment was detained using the prevailing commercial interest rates. Because it was a legal right, appellants did not have to ask or bargain for the payment of delay compensation. The court found that the agreement, after providing for the payment of the determined value of appellants' real and personal property was a clear and unambiguous statement that appellants were asserting their right to delay damages under § 611.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court and remanded this case for a hearing to determine the applicable rate of interest, the period of detention and amount of delay damages due to appellant condemnees on the unpaid balance of the determined value of the real and personal property taken by appellee condemnor.

Pennsylvania Nat'l Mut. Cas. Ins. Co. v. Department of Labor & Indus.


No. 217 C.D. 1995, No. 372 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


667 A.2d 753; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 539; 2 Wage & Hour Cas. 2d (BNA) 1713; 131 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P58,096


April 7, 1995, Argued  
November 22, 1995, Decided  
November 22, 1995, FILED

Stat. Ann. tit. 43, § 165-1 to 165-17, applied to an asbestos removal contract between petitioner city and the asbestos removal contractor.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, Pennsylvania Prevailing Wage Division, insurance company, city and city redevelopment authority, developed petitioner insurance company's new headquarters. Petitioner city contracted for asbestos removal. Petitioner prevailing wage division determined that the Pennsylvania Prevailing Wage Act, Pa. Stat. The act provided unions standing to contest wage issues, and since only two members of a seven member respondent board were unions, the due process complaint failed.

PPG Indus. v. Commonwealth


NO. 2355 C.D. 1987


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


681 A.2d 824; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 620


September 14, 1995, Argued  
November 3, 1995, Decided  
November 3, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Memorandum Opinion Redesignated Opinion and Ordered Published June 18, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. R-10,904. State Agency Board of Finance and Revenue.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner manufacturer sought review of a decision of the Board of Finance and Revenue (Pennsylvania), which upheld the resettlement by respondent, Pennsylvania Department of Revenue, of petitioner's capital stock tax.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner manufacturer, whose headquarters were in Pennsylvania, performed operations inside and outside of the state. Respondent, Pennsylvania Department of Revenue, conducted an audit of petitioner and concluded that it had improperly reported certain capital stock taxes. Respondent accepted the auditors' changes, and petitioner appealed the resettlement of the taxes. The State Board of Finance and Revenue (board) agreed with respondent's decision, and petitioner appealed. The court affirmed the decision of the board because petitioner failed to meet its burden in proving that the taxing statute in question, 72 P.S. § 7602(a), was unconstitutional. The court held that petitioner was afforded the choice under the "compute and pay" method as to how to compute its tax under § 602(b)(1), and having elected to compute its tax under the three-factor apportionment method, it was treated as if it were a foreign entity subject to the franchise tax under § 602(b)(1). The court concluded that the statute did not violate either the commerce clause or the equal protection clause and that it treated Pennsylvania corporations the same as foreign corporations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the State Board of Finance and Review, which upheld the resettlement by respondent, Pennsylvania Department of Revenue, of petitioner manufacturer's capital stock tax. Petitioner failed to meet its burden in proving that the taxing statute was unconstitutional. The taxing statute gave petitioner a choice as to how to compute its tax, and it did not violate the commerce clause or equal protection clause.

Garrett Group, L.P. v. County of Schuylkill


No. 3180 C.D. 1994


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


667 A.2d 255; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 480


September 14, 1995, ARGUED  
October 27, 1995, Decided  
October 27, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied March 25, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Pa. LEXIS 650.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. S-474-1944. Common Pleas Court of the county of Schuylkill. Judge DOLBIN.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of a judgment of the Common Pleas Court of Schuylkill County (Pennsylvania), which sustained the preliminary objections of appellees, county, townships, and school districts, finding that appellants failed to raise a substantial constitutional question in appellants' equity action for a tax refund and a reassessment of their surface and coal reserve properties.


OVERVIEW: In appellant taxpayers' equity action for a tax refund and a reassessment of their surface and coal reserve properties based on the constitutionality of The Fourth and Eighth Class County Assessment Law, 72 P.S. §§ 5453.101-5453.706, and The General County Assessment Law, 72 P.S. §§ 5020-1-5020-602, the trial court sustained the preliminary objections of appellees, county, townships, and school districts, finding that appellants failed to raise a substantial constitutional question. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the trial court did not have equity jurisdiction, because appellant taxpayers did not show the existence of a substantial question of constitutionality and the absence of an adequate statutorily prescribed remedy. Appellants did not aver that their properties were treated differently than other similar lands under either assessment law. Thus, their assertions were merely allegations of unconstitutionality and did not confer equity jurisdiction. Also, absent a question of constitutionality, the statutory procedures for reviewing alleged errors by taxing authorities was required to be pursued.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order sustaining the preliminary injunctions of appellees, county, townships, and school districts, holding that the trial court did not have equity jurisdiction, because appellant taxpayers did not aver a lack of uniformity with regard to other, similar properties subject to the assessment laws to establish a question of constitutionality, and that any redress had to occur through the statutory appeal procedures.

Sweeny v. State Bd. of Funeral Directors


No. 274 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


666 A.2d 1137; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 473


September 13, 1995, ARGUED  
October 26, 1995, Decided  
October 26, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEALED From No. 0339-48-93. State Agency State Board of Funeral Directors.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner funeral director appealed the order of the State Board of Funeral Directors (Pennsylvania), which revoked his funeral director's license subsequent to his having pled guilty to theft and conspiracy in the misappropriation of funds from an decedent's estate to which he had been named executor.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner funeral director misappropriated funds from the estate of a deceased. Petitioner pled guilty to theft and conspiracy charges. Subsequently, respondent State Board of Funeral Directors revoked petitioner's funeral director's license. Petitioner appealed the revocation based on a double jeopardy argument. The court affirmed the order of respondent. The court found that respondent's decision to revoke the license was based on respondent's duty to protect the public, and not a determination to deter or punish. The court found that petitioner held a position of trust in the community because he was a funeral director. The court reasoned that whether double jeopardy applied to administrative proceedings was based on the penalty imposed and the purposes it served. The court agreed that the revocation of the license was not unduly harsh because petitioner had been trusted with the deceased's estate and he breached that trust.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of respondent State Board of Funeral Directors that revoked petitioner funeral director's license. The court found that petitioner was not subjected to double jeopardy by the agency's revocation because the penalty was designed to protect the public from unscrupulous funeral directors. The punishment was not unduly harsh because petitioner had occupied a position of trust.

Adams Outdoor Advertising v. Borough of Stroudsburg


No. 205 C.D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


667 A.2d 21; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 477


September 14, 1995, ARGUED  
October 20, 1995, Decided  
October 20, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied May 30, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1205.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 3196 Civil 1993. Common Pleas Court of the county of Monroe. Judge O'BRIEN.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sign owners challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County (Pennsylvania), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent borough with regard to the tax upon off-premises advertisement.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sign owners filed a complaint for declaratory relief in the trial court, contending that respondent borough's ordinance, which taxed off-premises adverting, was invalid. The trial court found that the ordinance was not unconstitutional and entered judgment in favor of respondent and against appellees. The court affirmed the trial court judgment because there was nothing in the record or in the ordinance which suggested that the tax was directed at suppressing any particular ideas or that it was likely to stifle the free exchange of opinions. Moreover, because that tax was imposed upon all off-premises signs, it did not single out one small group of off-premises signs while exempting others from taxation. Further, appellants argued that the ordinance unconstitutionally imposed a content-based tax. The court disagreed because the ordinance imposed the tax on all off-premises signs regardless of their content, so the ordinance was content neutral. Further, the court found that the ordinance did not disadvantage non-commercial speech or violate appellants' rights to due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent borough with regard to its tax upon off-premises advertisement, because the tax did not violate appellant sign owners' constitutional rights.

Gwynedd Dev. Group v. Department of Labor & Indus.


No. 3017 C.D. 1994


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


666 A.2d 365; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 442


May 10, 1995, ARGUED  
September 27, 1995, Decided  
September 27, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted May 21, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1054.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 88-16055. State Agency, Bureau of Labor Standards.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, employer and shareholders, appealed a notice from respondent, the Department of Labor, to post bond or security pursuant to § 9.1(d) of the Wage Payment and Collection Law (WPCL), 43 P.S. § 260.9a(d), to secure assets for payment of money claimed by a former employee. Petitioners argued that respondent's action violated due process, was an abuse of discretion, and was a misinterpretation of the WPCL.


OVERVIEW: A former employee of petitioners, employer and shareholders, claimed that he was owed money by petitioners. After the employee filed a complaint in equity against petitioners, he requested that respondent, the Department of Labor, require petitioners to post bond as provided under § 9.1(d) of the Wage Payment and Collection Law (WPCL), 43 P.S. § 260.9a(d), to assure that payment of amounts owed could be made. Without a hearing, respondent directed petitioners to post a bond, and petitioners appealed that the notice violated their right to due process, was an abuse of discretion, and a misinterpretation of the WPCL. The court affirmed. The court held that petitioner's claim of a due process violation was premature because respondent had not levied execution on any property. Moreover, a reviewing court should not decide a constitutional question if the case can be decided on non-constitutional grounds. The court held that the WPCL was not unconstitutional because there were no standards to guide respondent's discretion. Respondent's reasoning in requiring petitioners to post a bond was not abuse of discretion. Therefore the court was required to affirm respondent's decision.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the action of respondent, the Department of Labor, which notified petitioners, employer and shareholders, that they were required to post a bond under the Wage Payment and Collection Law (WPCL), to secure assets possibly owed to a former employee who had filed a complaint against petitioners for monies owed for work performed. The court held that the WPCL was not unconstitutional and respondent had not abused its discretion.

OVERVIEW: Petitioner employee was dismissed for alleged patient abuse and sought review of the order of the civil service commission, which dismissed his appeal. Fornataro v. Workmen's Compensation Appeal Bd. (Cabot Corp.)


NO. 2751 C.D. 1994


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


663 A.2d 854; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 378


May 12, 1995, Submitted on Briefs  
August 11, 1995, Decided  
August 11, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied December 28, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Pa. LEXIS 1450.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. A94-0308. State Agency Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner claimant sought relief from a decision of respondent Pennsylvania Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board, which affirmed the decision of the referee that found that petitioner had not met the manifestations provision of the Pennsylvania Occupational Disease Act in making his claim for a work-related injury.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner claimant sought relief from the denial of his workmen's compensation claim and petition filed under §§ 301(c), 301(c)(2) of the Pennsylvania Occupational Disease Act (the Act), 77 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 1401(c), 1411(2), by the workmen's compensation appeal board (the board), which affirmed the decision of the referee that petitioner had not met the manifestations provisions of § 301(c)(2) by failing to file his claim within the 300 week period after the last date of employment. Petitioner argued that the time limits of the Act violated the equal protection clauses of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Pa. Const. art. III, § 18 and that no rational basis existed for requiring an arbitrary time limit within which a disease had to manifest itself. The court upheld the board's decision. Pa. Const. art. III, § 18 empowered the general assembly to allow time limits as long as the limits were alike for individuals and corporations. The Act met the constitutional rational-basis test because its classification was reasonably related to the legitimate state objective to prevent stale claims and speculation over whether a disease was work-related years after the exposure allegedly occurred.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the worker's compensation appeal board that denied petitioner claimant's request for compensation under the Pennsylvania Occupational Disease Act (the Act). The court held that the time provision requirements of the Act within which a claim must have been brought were constitutional and reasonably related to the legitimate state objective to prevent stale claims.

Bible v. Department of Labor & Indus.


No. 77 M. D. 1995


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


663 A.2d 837; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 398



August 4, 1995, Decided  
August 4, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Memorandum Opinion Redesignated Opinion and Ordered Published August 23, 1995.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner workers' compensation claimants and respondents, employers, state, state agency, and state official filed motions for summary judgment or judgment on the pleadings in petitioners' action which sought a declaration that a retroactivity provision in the Pennsylvania Workers' Compensation Act, § 306(c)(8), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77, § (513)(8), violated the contract and due process clauses of the Pennsylvania and United States Constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner workers' compensation claimants filed claims for compensation for loss of hearing against respondents, employers, state agency, and state official, and sought a determination that the retroactive application of an amendment to the Pennsylvania Workers' Compensation Act, § 306(c)(8), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77, § 513(8), violated the contract and due process clauses of the United States and Pennsylvania Constitutions. Petitioners and respondent employers filed motions for summary judgment, while respondents, state agency and state official, filed a motion for judgment on the pleadings or, in the alternative, summary judgment. The court granted petitioners' motion and denied respondents' motions. It determined that petitioners had a vested right to have their claims adjudicated under the prior version of § 513(8) because § 513(8), as it existed before the effective date of the amendment, was part of their employment contract and their causes of action clearly accrued before that date. The court reasoned that to have permitted retroactive application of the amended statute would have violated Pa. Const. art. I, § 17.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion for summary judgment of petitioner workers' compensation claimants and denied the motions for judgment on the pleadings or summary judgment of respondents, employers, state agency, and state official. It held that the retroactive application of an amended statute to petitioners' claims would have violated the Pennsylvania Constitution.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner claimant sought review of the decision of respondent, the Public School Employees' Retirement Board, which denied his request that his annuity be credited with 30 years of service. Respondent contended that petitioner knew he did not have 30 years and that PSERS had to correct its error.

The annual statements petitioner received erroneously credited him with the additional years. Prior to his retirement, petitioner confirmed with the Public School Employees' Retirement System (PSERS) that he had 30 years of service. It was not until after petitioner's retirement that PSERS discovered the error and reduced the monthly annuity benefits. Petitioner appealed the order of respondent, the Public School Employees' Retirement Board, which denied his appeal requesting that the annuity be calculated using 30 years and which ordered PSERS to collect the overpaid amounts. The court affirmed the decision. PSERS had to comply with the Public School Employees' Retirement Code, which required correction of errors. Petitioner should have known that he had not put in 30 years of service because of his sick leave.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed respondent, the Public School Employees' Retirement Board's, judgment, which denied petitioner claimant's request that his annuity be credited with 30 years of service. The court found that the Public School Employees' Retirement System (PSERS) had to comply with the Public School Employees' Retirement Code, which required the correction of errors made by PSERS. Petitioner knew there was an error in the calculation of the time.

Public Advocate & Consumers Educ. & Protective Ass'n v. City of Philadelphia


No. 3106 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


662 A.2d 686; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 329


May 8, 1995, Argued  
July 17, 1995, Decided  
July 17, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEALED From No. 2159 May Term, 1993. Common Pleas Court of the county of Philadelphia. Judge HERRON.
Public Advocate v. City of Philadelphia, 26 Phila. 527, 1993 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 152 (Pa. C.P., 1993)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public advocates challenged the order of the Common Pleas Court of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) which found in favor of respondent city and water department in refusing to set aside the water rates pursuant to Philadelphia, Pa., Ordinance § 1263 (1974) and the Bond Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 15901-924, for the fiscal years of 1993-1996.


OVERVIEW: Appellant public advocates filed a complaint in equity against respondent city and water department, seeking to enjoin implementation of the water rate. Appellants contended that the rate transferred excess interest earnings, which was an illegal payment in lieu of taxes that made the rate unjust and unreasonable. The lower court held that appellants waived their right to raise the illegal tax issue for failing to raise it at the rate hearings, but that the claim had merit because a clear nexus between customer payments and transferred funds was established. Appellants challenged this ruling, claiming that respondent's funds transfer pursuant to Philadelphia, Pa., Ordinance § 1263 (1974) and the Bond Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 15901-15924, was an illegal tax because customers received no service for the payment to the general fund from the excess interest of the sinking fund. The court affirmed the lower court's order, concluding that customers had no property interest in excess interest earnings, which were derived from the proceeds of bond sales, and that waiver could not be determined because appellants' complaint was not in the record.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment because the transfer of excess interest earnings to respondent city's general fund was not an unlawful tax. The application of revenues derived from the operation of respondent water department's utility to service bond debt conferred no property right on appellant public advocates to the excess interest earnings on the bond proceeds.

 

567 of 892 DOCUMENTS


West v. Hampton Township Sanitary Auth.


No. 1009 C.D. 1994, No. 1306 C.D. 1994, No. 1402 C.D. 1994


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


661 A.2d 459; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 309


March 14, 1995, Argued  
June 28, 1995, Decided  
June 28, 1995, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEALED From No. GD 91-12544. State Agency Common Pleas Court of the county of Allegheny.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which found in favor of appellees, township and sanitary authority, in a declaratory judgment action involving appellee sanitary authority's setting of certain water and sewage charges under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, §§ 301-322, 306A(t)(1). Appellee township filed a cross-appeal for an award of attorney's fees.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owners filed a declaratory judgment action challenging the tapping and connection fees associated with certain water and sewage charges set by appellees, township and sanitary authority. Appellants argued that the fees were improper under the Municipality Authorities Act of 1945, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, §§ 301-322, 306A(t)(1). The trial court found in favor of appellees but denied appellees an award of attorney's fees. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded. The court held that its scope of review was to examine whether the law was properly applied. Under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 306B(t)(1), it was not an abuse of discretion to include costs for the construction of facilities to correct peak hydraulic capacity problems related solely to existing users. However, the court remanded the issue of exclusion of costs involved in ammonia removal requirements. Appellee sanitary authority miscalculated appellants' water requirements, the facility's design capacity, and the connection fees, and the court remanded for recalculation. Appellee township was not entitled to attorney's fees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court which denied declaratory relief to appellant property owners in their action related to water and sewage fees. The court remanded for recalculation of the connection fees, appellants' water requirements, and the cost of ammonia removal. The court held that it was not an abuse of discretion to deny an award of attorney's fees to appellee township.

In re Appeal of Apgar


No. 2407 C.D. 1994


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


661 A.2d 445; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 293


April 4, 1995, Argued  
June 22, 1995, Decided  
June 22, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEALED From No. 1971-1993 and 4560-1993. Common Pleas Court of the county of Lancaster. Judge FARINA.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners challenged the ruling of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania) that affirmed the decision of the Zoning Hearing Board that denied appellant's challenge to the validity of an amendatory zoning ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owners abutted a tract of land subject to an amendatory zoning ordinance. Under the previous law, a water pumping station was not permitted on the property because of the setback lines. The local Board of Commissioners passed legislation allowing water pumping stations as a conditional use and reducing the required setbacks. The board then granted a conditional use permit for the water pumping station. Appellants challenged the ordinance, alleging that it was special legislation, and the trial court denied their claim. The court affirmed, holding that special legislation cases dealt with ordinances that were changed to prohibit a property owner from a previously acceptable use. The court declined to extend the law to apply to actions where the government enabled an owner to do more with its property. The court also held that the law was not unjustly discriminatory or arbitrary, the mindset of the board in creating the law was irrelevant, and the ordinance was related to the general welfare and common good.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order affirming the Zoning Hearing Board's decision, holding that the law of special legislation did not apply to the facts, the government agencies properly considered the ordinance, and the zoning change was related to the common good and general welfare of the community.

In re Petition for Referendum on Question of Amending Pittsburgh Home Rule Chapter

COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


660 A.2d 184; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 267


March 15, 1995, Argued  
June 5, 1995, Decided  
June 5, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEALED From No. GD-94-13204. Common Pleas Court of the county of Allegheny. Judge McLEAN.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant referendum petitioner sought review of a decision of the Common Pleas Court of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee objector's petition to set aside the referendum because it lacked the required number of signatures pursuant to 53 P.S. § 1-232 of the Home Rule Charter and Optional Plans Law (Charter) and because the referendum petition was untimely under 53 P.S. § 1-218 of the Charter.


OVERVIEW: Appellant referendum petitioner sought by referendum to transfer the bill paying functions of the Pittsburgh City Controller's office to the Mayor's office. Appellant filed the petition on the last possible day to have it placed on the ballot for the upcoming general election. Appellee objector filed a petition to set aside the referendum, and the trial court granted appellee's petition finding that the referendum did not contain the requisite number of elector's signatures under 53 P.S. § 1-232 of the Home Rule Charter and Optional Plans Law (Charter) and that the petition was untimely under 53 P.S. § 1-218 of the Charter because the referendum constituted a change in the form of government. The court dismissed the appeal as moot because the current election was completed and placing the referendum on the next available election violated § 1-232 due to the fact that the signatures were obtained well in excess of the 20 weeks prior to the next general election. The court found that the matter did not come under any of the exceptions to the mootness doctrine and that appellant's actions were a material factor in the delay of the case in reaching the court for review.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the appeal as moot because appellant referendum petitioner circulated his petition well beyond the 20 weeks of any forthcoming election. The court found that appellant's actions were a material factor in the delay in reaching the appellate court because he waited until the last possible day to file his petition and also delayed in bringing the appeal.

Verdecchia v. Unemployment Comp. Bd. of Review


No. 351 C.D. 1994, No. 406 C.D. 1994, No. 1120 C.D. 1994


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


657 A.2d 1341; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 176


March 13, 1995, Argued  
April 17, 1995, Decided  
April 17, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEALED From No. B-321516, B-321317, B-324007 and B-324008. State Agency, Unemployment Compensation Board of Review.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, four claimants whose cases were consolidated, appealed from various orders of respondent, Unemployment Compensation Board of Review (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the referees' decisions to deny petitioners unemployment compensation benefits under § 4(1)(4)(8) of the Unemployment Compensation Law, 43 P.S. § 753(1)(4)(8).


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, four claimants whose cases were consolidated, were employed by a church diocese at a personal care facility when the diocese decided to close the facility. When petitioners applied for unemployment compensation benefits, the referees denied them benefits under § 4(1)(4)(8) of the Unemployment Compensation Law, 43 P.S. § 753(1)(4)(8), and respondent, Unemployment Compensation Board of Review, affirmed. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that petitioners were ineligible for benefits because the personal care facility was operated by the diocese primarily for religious purposes, precluding them from receiving benefits under § 4(1)(4)(8). The court found that respondent had not erred in concluding that the diocese operated at the facility primarily for religious purposes. The facility was not a separately incorporated legal entity but, rather, was under the direct control of the diocese. Because the facility was operated primarily for religious purposes, petitioners were ineligible for benefits. The court also found that the diocese had good cause for its non-appearance at the initial referee's hearing because it had not received notice of the hearing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the various orders of respondent, Unemployment Compensation Board of Review, which affirmed the referees' decisions to deny petitioners, four claimants whose cases were consolidated, unemployment compensation benefits. The court held that petitioners were ineligible for benefits because the facility at which they were employed operated primarily for religious purposes and was under the direct control of the church diocese.

Stephens v. Pennsylvania State Bd. of Nursing


No. 17 C.D. 1994


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


657 A.2d 71; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 146


September 20, 1994, ARGUED  
March 28, 1995, Decided  
March 28, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied August 29, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Pa. LEXIS 649.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 0339-51-91. (File No. 89-51-07925. State Agency, State Board of Nursing.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner nurse appealed an order of respondent, Pennsylvania State Board of Nursing, which formally reprimanded petitioner and assessed a civil penalty pursuant to the Practical Nurse Law, 63 P.S. §§ 651-667.8, for allegedly permitting a nurse's aide to self-administer a controlled drug and not preventing the aide from abusing a patient at a nursing home where both were employed.


OVERVIEW: After the hearing examiner issued a proposed adjudication order that dismissed all charges against petitioner nurse for allegedly permitting a nurse's aide to self-administer a controlled drug and not preventing the aide from abusing a patient in the nursing home where both were employed, respondent, State Board of Nursing, reversed the hearing examiner. Respondent formally reprimanded petitioner for unprofessional conduct and assessed a civil penalty. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that 63 P.S. § 666(a)(8) of the Practical Nurse Law, 63 P.S. §§ 651-667.8, which gave authority to respondent to refuse, suspend, or revoke a nursing license for unprofessional conduct, was constitutionally sound. The standards of nursing conduct provided a definite warning to petitioner of what could have constituted unprofessional conduct by setting forth an ascertainable standard against which conduct could have been measured. The court found that respondent was not required to have made a separate finding that petitioner's conduct was of such a serious nature as to have required suspension or revocation of her license before ordering a formal reprimand for her actions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of respondent, State Board of Nursing, which formally reprimanded petitioner nurse and assessed a civil penalty for engaging in unprofessional conduct. The court held that the statute that gave respondent authority to revoke or suspend a nursing license was constitutionally sound because the standards of nursing conduct gave petitioner fair warning of what constituted unprofessional conduct.

Commonwealth v. CSX Transp.


No. 1127 C.D. 1994


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


653 A.2d 1327; 1995 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 11


November 1, 1994, ARGUED  
January 6, 1995, Decided  
January 6, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied October 13, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 3255 C 1991. State Agency, Common Pleas Court of the county of Westmoreland. Judge McCORMICK, JR.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth of Pennsylvania sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed the criminal information. Defendant transportation corporation was charged with violating the Fish and Game Code after allowing corn syrup to leak out of a derailed car into the nearby river, killing approximately 10,000 fish.


OVERVIEW: After a heavy rainfall and flooding, defendant transportation corporation's train tracks collapsed, and two trains derailed. As a result, one of the train cars broke open, and corn syrup leaked out and flowed into the nearby river, killing approximately 10,000 fish. Defendant was charged by information with violating the Fish and Game Code. The Pollution of Waters section of the Code imposed on the party with control over the harmful substance a duty to prevent it from entering commonwealth waters. Therefore, defendant was absolutely required while transporting hazardous materials not to allow those materials to enter the commonwealth's waters. The court found that the fact that the derailment was caused by rains and flooding did not relieve defendant of its duty to prevent the corn syrup from flowing into the river after the railcar broke open. The court reversed the order of the trial court, which dismissed the information filed against defendant, finding that because defendant's violation resulted in multiple fish being killed, subjecting it to a large fine did not mean that the individual penalties provided for in the applicable statute violated due process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order, which dismissed the information charging defendant transportation corporation with violating the fish and game code, because defendant was absolutely required by the applicable statute not to allow hazardous materials to enter commonwealth waters. The fact that the derailment was caused by flooding did not relieve defendant of its duty to prevent the hazardous substance from flowing into the river.

COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner challenged an order of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Delaware (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee school district's preliminary objections to appellant's counterclaim and dismissed appellant's counterclaim in an action arising for tax arrearages.


OVERVIEW: Appellee school district filed a complaint against appellant property owner for tax arrearages. The trial court sustained appellee's preliminary objections to appellant's counterclaim, which the court subsequently dismissed, and denied appellant's preliminary objections to appellee's objections. On review, the court affirmed. The court found the sustaining of appellee's objections, even though filed beyond the 20 day statutory period found in Pa. R. Civ. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order sustaining appellee's preliminary objections to appellant's counterclaim, the dismissal of appellant's counterclaim, and the denial of appellant's preliminary objections to appellee's preliminary objections. The court found that appellant did not have an absolute right to plead over, and a ruling on appellee's objections could be determined from the record.

Court of Common Pleas v. Pennsylvania Human Rels. Comm'n


No. 3033 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


653 A.2d 1312; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 703


June 8, 1994, ARGUED  
December 21, 1994, Decided  
December 21, 1994, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted June 13, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Pa. LEXIS 1722.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. Docket No. E-62881-D. State Agency, Pennsylvania Human Relations Commission.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant court sought review of an order by appellee, the Pennsylvania Human Relations Commission, which denied its motion to dismiss the action of appellee court employee for lack of jurisdiction based on separation of powers.


OVERVIEW: After appellee court employee was discharged by appellant court, he filed a complaint with appellee Pennsylvania Human Relations Commission (PHRC) alleging a violation of the Pennsylvania Human Relations Act (PHRA), 43 P.S. §§ 951-963, and seeking reinstatement. Appellant filed a motion to dismiss for lack of jurisdiction and appellee PHRC denied the motion. Reversing the decision of appellee PHRC, the court held that appellee PHRC lacked jurisdiction in the matter because the doctrine of separation of powers prohibited it from interfering with the judicial function of discharging personnel. The court reasoned that the discharge of a judicial employee was a judicial power vested by the United States Constitution in the courts, and that power could not constitutionally be policed, encroached upon, or diminished by another branch of government.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a decision of appellee Pennsylvania Human Relations Commission (PHRC) denying appellant court's motion to dismiss appellee court employee's action. The court found that appellee PHRC lacked jurisdiction of the matter. The court held that the doctrine of separation of powers prohibited appellee PHRC from interfering with the judicial function of discharging personnel.

Apgar v. State Employees' Retirement Sys.


No. 109 C.D. 1994


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


655 A.2d 185; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 705


September 21, 1994, Submitted on Briefs  
December 21, 1994, Decided  
December 21, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEALED From Order dated December 13, 1993. State Agency, Pennsylvania State Employes' Retirement Board.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee sought review of an order of the State Employees' Retirement Board (Pennsylvania), which discontinued her pension benefits from respondent, State Employee's Retirement System, pursuant to the Public Employee's Pension Forfeiture Act, 43 P.S. §§ 1311-1315, after petitioner pled guilty to a crime related to her public employment.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employee, a member of the State Employee's Retirement System, pled guilty to criminal conduct committed during the course of her employment. After notice and review, the State Employees' Retirement Board (board) ordered the discontinuance of her benefits as of the date of entry of the guilty plea pursuant to the Public Employee's Pension Forfeiture Act, 43 P.S. §§ 1311-1315 (Act 140). Petitioner filed an appeal seeking the reversal of the board's order alleging it retroactively applied Act 140. On review, the court affirmed the decision because the board's action was not retroactive and was well within its statutory authority granted under Act 140. The court found that petitioner's acceptance of her new job as supervisor triggered the statutorily mandated affirmation of the terms of employment whereby she assented to the terms set forth in Act 140. The court held Act 140 specifically included "rank and file" employees and, therefore, found that petitioner was bound by Act 140. The court found petitioner failed to present case law or legal argument to support her claim that the imposition Act 140 was a violation of her civil rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the State Employees' Retirement Board's decision to discontinue petitioner employee's pension benefits upon notice of a guilty plea to a crime committed during the course of her employment. The court held that petitioner was bound by the Public Employees' Pension Forfeiture Act (act) because although she was initially hired prior to the effective date of the act she assented to the act's terms upon her job promotion.

Harmon v. Mifflin County Sch. Dist.


NO. 876 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


651 A.2d 681; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 688


September 21, 1994, ARGUED  
December 15, 1994, Decided  
December 15, 1994, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication January 24, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEALED From No. 726 of 1992. Common Pleas Court of the County of Mifflin. Judge GATES, Senior Judge.



DISPOSITION: Vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Mifflin County (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee employee's challenge to his termination and remanded the matter to appellant for a second termination hearing.


OVERVIEW: Appellee employee was a custodian at a school. He was suspended without pay due to improper conduct. Appellant school district's board of directors accepted the recommendation that appellee be terminated. Appellant's solicitor was present at the meeting where appellant decided to terminate appellee. Appellee requested a hearing before the school board, and at the hearing appellant's solicitor represented appellant while counsel for the state school board association conducted the hearing. Appellee challenged the participation of appellant's solicitor as impermissible commingling of prosecutorial and adjudicative functions. The board denied the challenge and affirmed appellee's termination. The trial court held that there was impermissible commingling and remanded for a second termination hearing. It did not address appellee's claim that there was no substantial evidence to support the board's action. The court held that where a school solicitor either prosecuted a case or conducted a hearing as an advisor, but did not engage in both, there is no impermissible commingling. The court vacated the order and remanded for a hearing on appellee's substantial evidence claim.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order because there was no constitutional violation by appellant school district when its solicitor advised it on appellee employee's termination and then represented it at appellee's termination hearing. The court remanded for the trial court to address appellee's contention that appellant did not have substantial evidence for its findings.

In the alternative, the appellant sought a request for variance. Will v. Elec. Contractors Examining Bd.


No. 2451 C.D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


168 Pa. Commw. 535; 650 A.2d 1226; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 636


September 21, 1994, Submitted on Briefs  
November 23, 1994, Decided  
November 23, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 61-EQ-1990. State Agency, Common Pleas Court of the County of Erie. Judge JOYCE.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants electric company and electrical contractor sought review from a Common Pleas Court of the County of Erie (Pennsylvania) judgment affirming appellee electrical contractors examining board's suspension of appellant electrical contractor for gross negligence under the Erie Building Code, art. 17.13.02(1)(5), due to his installation of an incorrect wire.


OVERVIEW: While working for appellant electric company, appellant electrical contractor installed a 4160 volt wire on a transformer rated at only 600 volts. Appellee electrical contractors examining board then notified appellants that appellant electrical contractor's license would be suspended for 30 days because his act had been one of gross negligence under the Erie Building Code (Code), art. 1713.02(1)(5). The lower court affirmed the suspension because appellant electrical contractor had sufficient due process prior to the suspension. Appellants sought review, arguing that the Code was unconstitutional, vague, and overbroad insofar as it did not define what constituted acts of gross negligence. The court found that gross negligence was that which grossly deviated from the standard of care in question. Further, because the Code set forth the ordinary negligence standard, appellant electrical contractor's conduct, which grossly deviated from that standard, was gross negligence. Thus, the court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, holding that appellee had given proper due process and finding that appellant electrical contractor's conduct grossly deviated from the clearly specified standard for ordinary negligence; thus, the suspension was proper under the circumstances.

Crawford County Care Ctr. v. Workmen's Comp. Appeal Bd. (daly)


No. 781 C.D. 1994


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


168 Pa. Commw. 169; 649 A.2d 203; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 588


September 16, 1994, Submitted on Briefs  
October 24, 1994, Decided  
October 24, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied May 18, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Pa. LEXIS 2270.


PRIOR HISTORY: .APPEALED From No. A92-2463. State Agency, Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employer appealed from an opinion and order of the Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board (Pennsylvania) affirming, with a minor modification, the decision of the referee to grant in part and deny in part petitioner employer's petition to terminate, suspend, or modify the workmen's compensation benefits awarded to claimant employee.


OVERVIEW: Claimant began receiving total disability benefits for a work-related injury. Five months later, petitioner employer sought to terminate, suspend, or modify the benefits, alleging that claimant had returned to work. Petitioner had taken an automatic supersedeas pursuant to § 413(c) of the Workmen's Compensation Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77, § 774.2. The referee found that claimant had returned to work for three days when her total disability recurred. Because petitioner knew that claimant was no longer working at the time it filed its petition and took an automatic supersedeas, the referee assessed a penalty of 10 percent on the weekly benefits which should have been paid. Petitioner appealed the referee's decision. The finding that claimant had not fully recovered from her work injury, and that she was incapable of resuming her time-of-injury job was supported by substantial evidence. The modification of claimant's benefits was entirely proper given the testimony. Also, a penalty was properly imposed on petitioner for taking an automatic supersedeas because petitioner unilaterally terminated claimant's benefits for almost six months without written agreement or official order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order upholding the referee's decision in all respects because there was substantial evidence to support the referee's finding that claimant employee did not fully recover from her work injury, petitioner employer's unilateral suspension of benefits was in violation of claimant's rights, and the imposition of a penalty for violation of the statute was proper and well within the discretion of the referee.

584 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Garofolo v. Dep't of Revenue


No. 255 M.D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


167 Pa. Commw. 672; 648 A.2d 1329; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 567


October 5, 1993, Argued  
September 30, 1994, Decided  
September 30, 1994, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Application for Reargument Denied March 17, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Pa. LEXIS 174.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporation filed an action requesting a declaration that § 16 of Act 22, 72 P.S. §§ 7401-8296, which suspended operation of net loss carryover deductions from the taxable income of corporations, violated Pa. Const. arts. 1 and VIII, § 1. Petitioner filed a motion for summary judgment, which the court treated as a motion for judgment on the pleadings.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner corporation asked the court to declare that § 16 of Act 22, 72 P.S. §§ 7401-8296, which suspended operation of net loss carryover deductions from the taxable income of corporations, violated Pa. Const. arts. 1 and VIII, § 1. Respondent state filed an answer to petitioner corporation's action, denying that § 16 was unconstitutional. The court treated petitioner's motion for summary judgment as a motion for judgment on the pleadings. The court awarded judgment to respondent. According to the court, the statute did not violate the uniformity provision under art. VIII, § 1 because the state constitution did not require uniformity of tax burden over several years. The state tax base for that year was the same and the rate was unvarying for all corporations. Due process was not violated because a tax deduction was not a vested right of the taxpayer and was subject to repeal and suspension by the legislature at any time. The statute did not breach a quasi-contract because petitioner offered no proof that respondent was unjustly enriched by the petitioner's conduct of business in the state.


OUTCOME: The court awarded judgment on the pleadings to respondent state in petitioner corporation's action because the statute suspending operation of net loss carryover deductions from the taxable income of corporations did not violate either the uniformity provision or the due process clause of the state constitution.

Jaruszewicz v. Dep't of Envtl. Res.


No. 2707 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


167 Pa. Commw. 582; 648 A.2d 1285; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 552


June 6, 1994, ARGUED  
September 23, 1994, Decided  
September 23, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From Appeal No. 17558. State Agency, State Civil Service Commission.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee challenged the order of the State Civil Service Commission (Pennsylvania), which sustained her one-day suspension that was imposed by respondent state department of environmental resources for failing to tell the truth. Petitioner argued that her pre-disciplinary due process rights were violated and that the order upholding the suspension was not supported by substantial evidence.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employee disputed an order of the Civil Service Commission, which sustained her one-day suspension by respondent state department of environmental resources. The court reviewed a United States Supreme Court decision, a federal district court decision, and its own decision. They required some form of pre-disciplinary proceedings in either termination or suspension situations. The court held that petitioner could not be deprived of her property right to continued employment without satisfying her pre-disciplinary due process rights. Because she was a civil servant employed in the classified civil service as governed by the Pennsylvania Civil Service Act, she could not be suspended without being afforded pre-disciplinary due process as set forth by decisions from the federal and state supreme courts. Respondent deprived her of the opportunity to respond to the complaint, because it was indicated to her that she would have an opportunity at a later meeting, which was never held. The court reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed. Petitioner employee could not be deprived of her property right to continued employment by suspension from work without satisfying her pre-suspension due process rights. Petitioner was deprived of that right without giving her the opportunity to fully respond to the complaint against her by indicating to her that she would have an opportunity to respond at a later meeting, which was never held.

Precision Equities v. Franklin Park Borough Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 2574 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


166 Pa. Commw. 607; 646 A.2d 756; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 468


June 8, 1994, Argued  
August 16, 1994, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied January 9, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Pa. LEXIS 1595.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. S.A. 3997 - 92. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Judge McLean.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) that affirmed a decision of appellee zoning hearing board denying appellant's challenge to appellee municipality's zoning code.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowner filed a challenge to the validity of a zoning code, pursuant to 53 P.S. § 10916.1, and contended that the code did not provide for a fair share of small residential lots and was unconstitutionally exclusionary. The court noted that the parties agreed that the community was a logical area for development and its present level of development did not preclude further development. The court stated that the question then became focused on determining the exclusionary impact of the zoning ordinance. Appellant argued that the zoning ordinance failed to make an adequate provision for single-family detached housing for low-to-moderate income families, which appellant equated with discrimination against minorities. The court stated that under Pennsylvania law, appellee municipality was only required to provide a fair share of various housing types, it was not required to provide a range of lot sizes for single-family housing. Therefore, appellant's challenge failed. Appellant's challenge on equal protection grounds also failed because appellant failed to show racially discriminatory intent or purpose.


OUTCOME: The order of the lower court which affirmed the denial of appellant landowner's challenge to appellee municipality's zoning code was affirmed because appellant failed to demonstrate that appellee municipality failed to provide a fair share of dwelling types.

Morganelli v. Casey

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent governor filed a motion to open the peremptory judgment in mandamus granted to petitioner district attorney, which directed respondent to issue execution warrants in two criminal cases involving death sentences.


OVERVIEW: Respondent failed to issue execution warrants in two criminal cases where death sentences were imposed for a number of years. Respondent filed a motion to open the peremptory judgment in mandamus, questioning whether the issuance of the execution warrants was at his discretion and not subject to mandamus order. The court denied respondent's motion ruling that his inaction or inordinate delay constituted a breach of his statutory duty to take care that the laws were faithfully executed. The court concluded that there was no basis to reopen the mandamus judgment, noting that respondent had sufficient time to issue the warrants. The court lifted the stay and ordered respondent to issue a death warrant within 30 days of the court's order.


OUTCOME: The court denied respondent governor's motion to open the peremptory judgment because respondent had more than enough time to issue the warrants. The court lifted the stay and ordered respondent to issue a death warrant within 30 days from the order.

Larsen v. Senate of Pa.


No. 317 M.D. 1994


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


166 Pa. Commw. 472; 646 A.2d 694; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 452


August 2, 1994, ARGUED  
August 5, 1994, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state supreme court judge sought a preliminary injunction enjoining an impeachment trial under Pa. Const. art. VI, § 4 by respondents state senate impeachment trial committee members.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner state supreme court judge sought a preliminary injunction enjoining respondents state senate impeachment trial committee members from conducting an impeachment trial under Pa. Const. art. VI, § 4, in connection with petitioner's misbehavior in office resulting in criminal proceedings against him. The court first determined the issue of whether the case involved justiciable questions had to be resolved by resort to interpreting the text of the state and U.S. Const. The court reasoned that it was barred from intervening with the impeachment process in the present unique case where petitioner sought an exercise of prior restraint upon the senate and its adjudicative impeachment function. The court found the constitutional protection against double jeopardy was inapplicable when the impeachment process clearly did not displace criminal liability; impeachment could never be construed as a charge to which there might have been a criminal court conviction. The court found it lacked power to intervene in advance of legislative action, and petitioner failed to establish the alleged manifest wrong or that his right to legal relief was clear for purposes of an injunction.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner state supreme court judge's motion for injunctive relief enjoining respondents state senate impeachment trial committee members from conducting an impeachment hearing because the court lacked the power to intervene in advance of the legislative adjudicative process and petitioner did not establish the manifest wrong or right to legal relief for purposes of entitlement to a preliminary injunction.

In re Petition to Increase Millage Limit Levied on Real Estate from 25 Mills to 30 Mills


NO. 2971 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


166 Pa. Commw. 161; 646 A.2d 61; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 412; 646 Pa. 61


May 11, 1994, ARGUED  
July 20, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 93-1109. Common Pleas Court of Perry County. Judge KURTZ.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of the order of the Common Pleas Court of Perry County (Pennsylvania), that granted appellee Perry County Board of Commissioners' petition to increase property tax millage. Appellants argued that 16 P.S. § 1770 was unconstitutional because it gave unfettered discretion to the judiciary to set the tax rate, and it was unconstitutionally vague because it did not delineate a clear standard of review.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayers sought review of the order that granted appellee Perry County Board of Commissioners' petition to increase property tax millage. The court denied appellee's motion to dismiss for failure to comply with Pa. R. Civ. P 235 because appellants complied with Pa. R. Civ. P. 521(a) and the attorney general did not seek intervention in the appeal or raise the lack of notice as his reason for not intervening. The court affirmed the order because appellants did not overcome the presumption that 16 P.S. § 1770 was constitutional. The court held that § 1770 did not grant the judiciary the power to tax because it could only review appellee's decision. The court held that § 1770 was not vague because the plain language of § 1770 provided discretion in the trial court to determine whether due cause existed to warrant additional millage without giving it the authority to review the wisdom of the underlying legislative decision. The court held that the due cause standard was sufficiently specific to guide the trial court's review.


OUTCOME: The order that granted appellee Perry County Board of Commissioners' petition to increase property tax millage was affirmed. Appellant taxpayers failed to rebut the presumption of the constitutional validity of the governing statute. The governing statute required the trial court to apply a specific due cause standard to its review of appellee's petition and gave the trial court no authority to set the tax rate.

Robison v. Fish & Boat Comm'n


No. 1535 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


166 Pa. Commw. 97; 646 A.2d 43; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 408


June 9, 1994, Argued  
July 18, 1994, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Application for Reargument Denied September 8, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 525. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied January 9, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Pa. LEXIS 1629.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. FC R92-1. State Agency, Pennsylvania Fish and Boat Commission.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner boatowner sought review of an order of respondent Pennsylvania Fish and Boat Commission, which found no violation of either the Equal Protection Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1, or the Uniformity Clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1, in its raising of the two-year boat registration fee authorized by 30 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5308 and 5104.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner boatowner paid a two-year boat registration fee in 1992 after the legislature had increased the annual fee from $ 6 to $ 15 under 30 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5104. Petitioner brought a purported class action against respondent Pennsylvania Fish and Boat Commission, asserting that persons paying their fee for 1992-1993 were discriminated against as compared to those paying for 1991-1992. Respondent found that its staggered two-year system was proper under 30 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5308, and petitioner appealed. The court, however, affirmed the ruling, finding first that the fee was not a tax and that the Uniformity Clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1, did not apply. The court further opined that, even if it did, neither it nor the Equal Protection Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1, had been violated by the raising of the fee or its implementation in a two-year system because the legislature had the power to increase the fees, and respondent had the rational reason of reducing administrative costs when it implemented the two-year system.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the ruling of respondent Pennsylvania Fish and Boat Commission in which it found no violation of equal protection or the tax uniformity clause in charging petitioner boatowner more for a 1992-1993 registration than previous owners were charged for their 1991-1992 registrations. A rational basis existed for the difference in fees.

Fid. Bank, N.A. v. Commonwealth by & ex rel. Dep't of Revenue


No. 356 M.D. 1989


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


165 Pa. Commw. 524; 645 A.2d 452; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 362


April 13, 1994, ARGUED  
July 7, 1994, FILED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner bank and respondents, commonwealth and governor, filed post-trial motions pursuant to Pa. R. Civ. P. 227.1, to a decree nisi granting petitioner's request for a refund of improperly paid bank shares taxes.


OVERVIEW: Respondent commonwealth incurred a shortfall in budget expectations when a tax on banks was declared unconstitutional and respondent was required to repay amounts collected under the invalid tax. A decree nisi was issued granting petitioner bank's request for a refund of improperly paid bank share taxes. Respondent issued credits to banks impacted and imposed a different tax in order to attempt to recoup its shortfall. Petitioner challenged the validity of both the credit in lieu of a refund and the imposition of the new tax. The court found held that recoupment of an unplanned shortfall in state tax receipts was a legitimate legislative concern. The court also found that respondent's method of averaging bank values over a period of years was a reliable method of determining value. A tax credit that was available only to banks which were chartered after a set date was found by the court to be unconstitutional. Creation of a tax class based solely upon the date that an entity was created violated uniformity of taxation. Because that tax credit portion of the code sought to be enacted was severable, only those sections of the code tied to the provision were stricken.


OUTCOME: Tax credits in lieu of a refund were held to be a valid method of resettling unconstitutionally assessed tax amounts against petitioner bank. Respondent commonwealth had a legitimate legislative concern in attempting to recoup an unexpected shortfall in tax monies. Creation of a tax class based upon the date of entity charter was contrary to uniformity of taxes and was therefore invalid.
