Henning v. Watanabe (in Re Private Rd. in E. Rockhill Township)


No. 1573 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


165 Pa. Commw. 240; 645 A.2d 313; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 325


May 9, 1994, ARGUED  
June 21, 1994, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied December 1, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 28 M.R. 1988 (18). Common Pleas Court of Bucks County. Judge RUFE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners sought review of the order from the Court of Common Pleas, Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a decision to lay out a private road across appellants' property pursuant to the Private Road Act, 36 P.S. §§ 2731-2891.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowners challenged the decision of the Board of Views (Board), which recommended laying out a private road across their property pursuant to the Private Road Act (Act), 36 P.S. §§ 2731 - 2891. The trial court denied appellants' post-trial motions and confirmed the Board's recommendation, holding that the statute did not authorize the taking of private property for private uses but was a constitutional use of eminent domain power. On appeal, appellant challenged the taking for both constitutional and substantive reasons. The court held that establishing roads to open landlocked property had public as well as private uses and that the Act was not unconstitutional. The court held that the Board made specific findings of fact that the properties were landlocked and that, therefore, a private road was necessary and that, after taking all relevant things into account, the road should have been opened, as it was an established roadbed and its use required significantly less construction or maintenance and caused the least harm to the properties. The court affirmed the trial court's order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order from the trial court that affirmed a decision to lay out a private road across appellant landowners' property pursuant to the Private Road Act. The court held that the decision had public as well as private uses, that a private road was necessary, and that the road was an established roadbed, the construction of which would have caused the least harm to the properties.

 

598 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Danishefsky v. Commonwealth


No. 20 M.D. 1994


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


165 Pa. Commw. 223; 645 A.2d 305; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 314


May 9, 1994, ARGUED  
June 20, 1994, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Application for Clarification Granted July 19, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 421. As Amended July 19, 1994.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state trooper association filed a writ of mandamus seeking a declaratory judgment that Act 44 of 1993, § 8(f.1)(3)(vi), effectively denied full reimbursement of prescription costs incurred from work-related injuries.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner state trooper association filed an action for mandamus seeking a declaratory judgment that members who suffered work-related injuries were effectively denied reimbursement for prescription costs under Act 44 of 1993, § 8(f.1)(3)(vi), and that the denial constituted a violation of its members' due process rights. Respondent, Department of Labor and Industry, filed a demurrer alleging that petitioner failed to state a cause of action. The court held that even if some of petitioner's members had paid for prescriptions for which they had not been reimbursed fully, the language of § 8 imposed no individual liabilities to employees and in no way impaired petitioner's members' rights to have prescriptions paid for by respondent and consequently did not violate the contract clauses of the United States and Pennsylvania Constitutions. The court sustained respondent's preliminary objections and dismissed petitioner's petition for review.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed petitioner state trooper association's writ of mandamus seeking a declaratory judgment that Act 44 of 1993 effectively denied full reimbursement of prescription costs incurred from work-related injuries. The court held that the language of the statute did not impose individual liability on petitioner's members nor did it impair members' rights to have prescriptions paid for by respondent, Department of Labor and Industry.

 

599 of 892 DOCUMENTS


McLaughlin v. Dep't of Transp.


No. 3067 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


165 Pa. Commw. 169; 644 A.2d 288; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 310


May 6, 1994, Submitted On Briefs  
June 17, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 1097 C.D. 1993. Common Pleas Court of Mercer County. Judge FRAMPTON


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commonwealth sought review of the decision of the Common Pleas Court of Mercer County (Pennsylvania). The issue before the court was whether a motor vehicle license suspension is legally imposed if a person pleading guilty to a violation of the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device and Cosmetic Act, 35 P.S. §§ 780-101 to 781-144, is not informed that such a plea will result in a license suspension.


OVERVIEW: Before the court was whether a motor vehicle license suspension is legally imposed if a person pleading guilty to a violation of the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device and Cosmetic Act, 35 P.S. §§ 780-101 to 781-144, is not informed that such a plea will result in a license suspension. The lower court's decision was reversed. The court held that appellee's license suspension was a collateral civil consequence of a criminal conviction and as such was legally imposed despite the lack of knowledge of this consequence at the time appellee entered a guilty plea. Therefore, the license suspension imposed on appellee as a collateral consequence of his violation of the act was both valid and legal. The two consecutive 90-day suspensions of operating privileges issued by the department of transportation were reinstated.


OUTCOME: The lower court's decision was reversed. The court held that appellee's license suspension was a collateral civil consequence of a criminal conviction and as such was legally imposed despite the lack of knowledge of this consequence at the time appellee entered a guilty plea.

 

600 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Plumstead Township v. Dep't of Transp.


No. 2783 C.D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


164 Pa. Commw. 514; 643 A.2d 786; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 288


May 9, 1994, ARGUED  
June 3, 1994, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner township challenged the order of respondent Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, which found in favor of a paving company and affirmed the granting of a highway occupancy permit.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner township appealed the order of respondent Pennsylvania Department of Transportation (DOT) that dismissed petitioner's exception to respondent's final report granting a highway occupancy permit to a paving company. On appeal, petitioner argued that respondent's findings that the paving company had provided for an adequate intersection, which was required by the permit, were not supported by substantial evidence. The court found that respondent's issuance of the permit was a reasonable exercise of its regulatory power. Furthermore, respondent had the authority to issue permits for the opening of driveways onto state highways. The court found that it could not hold that respondent lacked substantial evidence to issue the permit to the paving company. The hearing officer, as fact-finder, determined credibility of witnesses including experts, and the fact that he chose to believe one over the other did not make his finding capricious or demonstrate that he deliberately ignored evidence. Therefore, the court found that respondent did not err in issuing the permit.


OUTCOME: The decision of respondent Pennsylvania Department of Transportation that found in favor of a paving company and affirmed the granting of a highway occupancy permit was affirmed. The hearing referee was the sole finder of fact in determining the credibility of witnesses, and there was no indication that his decision was capricious or that he deliberately ignored the evidence.
